Prologue

I met Leandro — Nanopi to his friends — more than fifteen years ago, at one of those endless gatherings at a bar in Córdoba. I remember someone mentioned Borges and what should have been a passing comment turned into a two-hour exploration of infinity, labyrinths, and the nature of time. Leandro listened with an intensity that bordered on uncomfortable, and when he spoke, he did so as if he were putting together pieces of a puzzle that only he could see.

That's how he is. Leandro doesn't read books: he inhabits them. He underlines them with fury, discusses them with anyone who sits down to drink mate, abandons them for weeks and returns to them like someone returning to an unfinished conversation. That silent obsession is what led him, over years, to build this Personal Pantheon without even knowing he was doing it.

The book you hold in your hands is not an encyclopedia of famous thinkers. It's a map of the emotional and intellectual treasure of someone who decided to take seriously the question of how to think, not what to think. Each of these sixteen names represents a conversation Leandro had with himself, a crack through which a little more light entered.

I've seen him recommend Taleb at a dinner, quote Marcus Aurelius in a difficult moment, and get emotional — yes, emotional — explaining why Feynman was a genius not despite his irreverence, but because of it. This book is Leandro in pure form: rigorous but warm, profound but accessible, personal but universal.

Enjoy the journey. And if at the end you find yourself building your own pantheon, don't be surprised. That's exactly what this book aims to provoke.

— A friend

Introduction

A few years ago, someone asked me what books I'd take to a desert island. It's a trick question, because the answer is never about the books — it's about who you want nearby when everything else disappears. In my case, the answer wasn't books. It was people. Or more precisely, the ideas of certain people who, without knowing me, taught me how to think.

That's how this Personal Pantheon was born.

The idea is simple: we all have mentors we never met. People who lived in other times, who spoke other languages, who wrote for other audiences, but whose words reach us as if they were written for us. Curating a list of sixteen thinkers was not an academic exercise. It was an act of gratitude and clarity: putting in order those who helped me put my own head in order.

The list wasn't born complete. At first it was a chaos of names, some obvious, others embarrassing, many provisional. I moved them around like chess pieces, seeing who conversed best with whom, what ideas resonated, which contradicted each other in productive ways. Over time, three concentric circles emerged, three levels of depth.

Tier 1 — Foundational. Borges, Feynman, Taleb, Deutsch, Thiel. These are the ones who changed my way of seeing the world at a fundamental level. With Borges I learned that reality is a text we can rewrite. With Feynman, that methodical doubt is not weakness but a superpower. Taleb vaccinated me against noise. Deutsch gave me a theory of everything that matters. Thiel showed me that thinking from scratch is possible.

Tier 2 — Epistemology. Popper, Munger, Kahneman, Graham, Yudkowsky. These are the craftsmen of the most important tool: how to know if something is true. Popper taught me to seek what refutes my ideas, not what confirms them. Munger and Kahneman vaccinated me against biases I didn't even know I had. Graham showed me that clarity is a moral value. Yudkowsky, that the universe is big and our mind is small, but we can expand it.

Tier 3 — Practical wisdom. Marcus Aurelius, Epictetus, and Seneca (the Stoics), Alan Watts, Krishnamurti, Facundo Cabral. These didn't teach me to think better but to live better. The Stoics gave me a manual for difficult days. Watts reconciled me with the fact that I'm not in control. Krishnamurti shook me with the uncomfortable question. And Facundo Cabral — yes, the singer-songwriter — reminded me that street wisdom is worth as much as book wisdom.

Each chapter of this book is dedicated to one of these sixteen thinkers. They don't go in order of importance, but in the order I encountered them. And each chapter has the same structure: who they are, what they said, why it matters, and how to apply it to real life. No unnecessary jargon or academic pretensions. This is not a thesis. It's a conversation.

One warning: this is not a book to agree with everything it says. If after reading the Taleb chapter you want to argue with him, perfect. If Krishnamurti strikes you as insufferable, welcome. The goal is not for you to adopt my pantheon, but for you to build your own.

Because in the end, that's what it's about. Surrounding yourself with the voices that help you think more clearly, live with more purpose, doubt with more grace. My Personal Pantheon is my imaginary tribe. I invite you to meet them, debate them, question them. And if some stay, let them stay. If all leave, let them leave.

But don't end up without your own.

— Leandro Pisaroni (Nanopi)

Chapter 1Jorge Luis Borges

Life: Jorge Francisco Isidoro Luis Borges Acevedo (1899–1986), writer, librarian, blind man, and, by his own account, "a citizen of Geneva, Buenos Aires, and the universe." He was born in Buenos Aires into a family with its own library — his father, Jorge Guillermo Borges, was a lawyer, psychologist, and English teacher, and his mother, Leonor Acevedo Suárez, descended from one of Argentina's founding families. His father's library contained all the classics of English literature, and Borges learned to read in English before Spanish — in fact, his first book was Don Quixote in English, and when he discovered it existed in Spanish, it seemed to him an unfaithful translation. That early experience (an original that is a copy of a copy, one language seeping into another, fidelity as illusion) planted the obsessions he would cultivate his whole life: time, infinity, identity, the library, the book, and the labyrinth.

At 15, his family moved to Europe — first to Geneva, where Borges studied at the Collège Calvin and learned French and German; then to Spain, where he came into contact with Ultraísmo, an experimental poetic movement that taught him literature was not a craft of preservation but of radical invention. He returned to Buenos Aires in 1921 at 22, already formed as a European who now had to discover his country from the outside — that fissure (being Argentine from the desert, from exile, from his father's library that smelled of English) gave him an oblique gaze that never left him. He began publishing essays and poems in literary magazines, founded several (Prisma, Proa), and moved in Buenos Aires' avant-garde circles — but always from the margins, more observer than participant. He never wrote a novel. He never wanted to. It wasn't that he couldn't: it was that the form struck him as impure, too tied to psychology, context, and custom. Borges preferred the short story, the essay, the false review, the poem — genres that don't demand filling pages but condensing worlds.

His public life began late: at 38 he published A Universal History of Infamy (1935), his first book that wasn't a poetry collection or loose essay. At 45 he published Ficciones (1944), which catapulted him to international recognition. At 46, a blow to the head during the 1946 flu pandemic nearly killed him — and paradoxically, that brush with death sharpened his writing. In 1955, he was named director of the National Library, a post he had always dreamed of — but by then he was already going blind, like his father and grandfather before him. The irony was not lost on him: "to have received the two greatest gifts a man can receive: the library and blindness." He never left the Library, and yet his work travels through all centuries and all continents. He is not a writer of literature: he is a writer of ideas who uses literature as a vehicle. As well-read as no one else, it is said that when a text cites Borges, Borges has already read what the text cites. He died in Geneva, the city of his adolescence, at 86.

Core Ideas:

  1. Infinity as a concrete labyrinth — Borges does not use infinity as an abstract mathematical concept, but as a concrete and terrifying experience. The Library of Babel is infinite and therefore a prison: it contains every possible book, but that very totality renders any individual search absurd and tragic. The Aleph is a point of infinitesimal diameter that contains all points of the universe — to see it is to see everything, and whoever sees it cannot communicate what they saw because language has no coordinates for the total. The garden of forking paths is not a metaphor for choice: it is the literal description of a universe where all possibilities coexist. Borgesian infinity is not liberating; it is vertiginous. It confronts you with the fact that there is more than you will ever be able to grasp — and that this disproportion is not an accident, it is the nature of reality. The question is not "how to reach the end" but "how to choose a path knowing that all paths exist and that most of the ones you didn't take are also yours." This idea is the most precise literary prefiguration of the many-worlds interpretation of quantum mechanics, but Borges did not find it in physics — he found it in Ts'ui Pên's story.
  1. Fiction is knowledge (not entertainment) — For Borges, telling a story is not a form of distraction: it is a form of knowing. The paradox of Achilles and the Tortoise is not understood by analyzing it mathematically — it is understood by feeling it in the story where Achilles never catches the tortoise because the language of the story embodies the paradox. "The South" does not explain Argentine identity — it embodies it: the story of a man who dies or dreams he dies in the place he should never have left. "The Library of Babel" is not an essay on the epistemology of information overload — it is the experience of being lost in a library where there is too much to read. Fiction is the most powerful vehicle for truths that analytical prose cannot touch because analytical prose demonstrates, but fiction shows. Metaphor does not embellish: it reveals. A concept that can only be understood as direct experience is a concept philosophy cannot exhaust. Borges is to epistemology what Escher is to geometry: he does not explain the problem, he puts you inside it.
  1. Time as coexistence and not as arrow — Borges works with at least three conceptions of time throughout his work, and he commits to none. There is linear time (Heraclitus, the river, one does not bathe twice, irreversible loss). There is circular time (Nietzsche, the Eternal Return, the wheel that turns again, repetition as the structure of destiny — "The Immortal" where the immortal is all men and no one, and his life is an infinite circle). And there is branching time (the garden of forking paths, where each decision creates a branch, and all branches coexist). What is fascinating about Borges is not that he chooses one — it's that he holds all three, knowing they are contradictory, and uses that contradiction as creative fuel. For Borges, time is not an arrow advancing in one direction — it is an infinite delta where all possibilities exist simultaneously. In "Time and J. W. Dunne," Borges explores the idea that time is a river flowing in both directions, and that past and future are equally real. This vision anticipates the many-worlds interpretation (Deutsch), the theory of parallel universes, and hypotheses of non-linear time. It is not poetry — it is concrete metaphysics.
  1. The self is a necessary fiction — In "Borges and I," Borges divides himself in two. The Borges who writes (the public author, who goes to lectures, who signs books) and the Borges who walks through Buenos Aires, who falls in love, who gets bored, whose anecdotes are private. The first is known; the second is the one who knows. But they are not two different people — they are the same person, split by language. "I am not sure which of the two is writing this page." This fissure is central to all his work: the self is not a stable, coherent identity — it is a construction, a fiction we need to function but that does not withstand deep scrutiny. In "The Aleph," Beatriz Viterbo is dead and Borges reconstructs her in his memory — but that "Beatriz" is a fiction he himself has created, not the real person. In "The Shape of the Sword," the narrator turns out to be the traitor and the hero is the other — but "the other" is himself. In "Theme of the Traitor and the Hero," a traitor is executed by patriots, but the story reveals that he himself organized his own execution to serve the cause — the traitor is the hero, betrayal is loyalty. The Borgesian self is not a unity: it is a battlefield where identities cross, negate, and contain each other. This idea connects directly with Krishnamurti (the self as illusory construction), with Buddhist philosophy (anatta, non-self), and with modern psychology (the narrative self).
  1. The false quotation as a machine of truth — Borges invented entire books, cited authors who did not exist (Herbert Quain, Pierre Menard, Ts'ui Pên), attributed ideas to philosophers who never said them, wrote reviews of books no one had ever written. His defense: if the idea is interesting, whether it is true or not is secondary. The false quotation is not a deception — it is a legitimate resource for producing knowledge. When Borges quotes "a Chinese sage" who said that "dreams are real as long as they last," it does not matter that the Chinese sage does not exist: the idea is true, and the fictional author gives it a form that makes it memorable. This device is profoundly epistemological: truth does not depend on the authority of the source but on the quality of the idea. A false idea spoken by a real author is less valuable than a true idea spoken by a fictional author. Borges decenters the authority of the text and places it on the idea itself. This resonates with Taleb's notion that origin matters less than the robustness of the idea, and with Deutsch's view that good explanations stand on their own, regardless of who says them.
  1. The original does not exist — only readings exist — In "Pierre Menard, Author of the Quixote," Borges presents a 20th-century French writer who decides to write the Quixote anew — not to copy it or translate it, but to rewrite it word for word from his own historical experience. The result: the same chapters, the same words as Cervantes, but infinitely richer because the context has changed. "Cervantes' text and Menard's are verbally identical, but the second is almost infinitely richer." This idea destroys the notion of an original work: if two identical texts have different meanings depending on who reads them and when, then meaning does not reside in the text — it resides in the relationship between the text and its reader. There is no original work, only original readings. Every reader rewrites the book they read. This is not relativism (anything goes) — it is recognizing that interpretation is a creative act, not a passive one. The author is not the owner of meaning — he shares it with every reader who approaches the text. This idea connects with reception theory in literature, but Borges took it further: if meaning changes with the reader, then time itself rewrites texts. Homer is not the same after Virgil, nor Virgil after Dante, nor Dante after Joyce. Books live and die according to who reads them.
  1. Knowledge is a labyrinth, not a ladder — Borges rejects the idea that knowledge accumulates linearly (the ladder of progress). For him, knowledge is a labyrinth that forks, closes in on itself, has dead ends and unexpected shortcuts. In "The Library of Babel," the librarians spend their lives searching for the book that contains the key to all other books — but that book does not exist, or it exists but is indistinguishable from all the rest. In "Death and the Compass," Detective Lönnrot constructs a perfect theory that leads him directly into the trap where the criminals await him: his knowledge is perfect and it leads him to his death. Knowledge is not a key that opens doors — sometimes it is the door that closes behind you. Borges reminds us that knowing more is not always better: sometimes knowing more gets you more deeply lost. Erudition without wisdom is not knowledge — it is a larger labyrinth. This idea is a warning for the investor: data will not save you, information will not protect you, and exhaustive analysis can be the most elegant distraction of all.
  1. The universe as a book that reads itself — One of Borges' deepest obsessions is the idea that the universe is intelligible because it is written in some language — but that language is inaccessible or contradictory. In "The Library of Babel," the universe is a library and meaning is in some book no one has read. In "The Analytical Language of John Wilkins," Borges discovers a Chinese classification of animals that is absurd ("belonging to the Emperor," "embalmed," "that from a distance look like flies") and shows that every classification system is arbitrary. In "Funes the Memorious," Funes remembers everything but cannot think because thinking requires generalizing and forgetting — knowledge requires forgetting, requires loss. Borges suggests that the universe has meaning, but that meaning is not in a single book or a single language — it is in the tension between all possible languages. Knowledge is not finding the book that explains everything — it is understanding why that book cannot exist. This idea is the most Popperian of Borges (though prior to Popper): knowledge advances not by accumulating truths but by awareness of our limitations.

Key Works:

  • Ficciones (1944) — His most important book and the mandatory entry point. Contains "The Garden of Forking Paths," "The Library of Babel," "Pierre Menard, Author of the Quixote," "The Circular Ruins," "Death and the Compass," and "The South." Each story is a thinking machine: a philosophical concept (infinity, branching time, authorship, dream and reality) presented as narrative. There is not a weak story in this book. If you read only one book by Borges, let it be this one. Read it slowly, one story per day, and let each one inhabit you for 24 hours before moving to the next.
  • The Aleph (1949) — The companion to Ficciones. If Ficciones is the book of intellectual infinity, The Aleph is the book of emotional infinity. Contains "The Aleph" (the famous story of the point that contains all points), "The Immortal," "The Theologians," "Story of the Warrior and the Captive," "The Writing of the God," and "Emma Zunz." It is a denser and sadder book than Ficciones — Borges had lost his mother (emotionally, not physically) and had gone through a love crisis. The stories in The Aleph are longer, more complex, and more desperate. If Ficciones is the Borges of intellectual wonder, The Aleph is the Borges of metaphysical melancholy. Read it after Ficciones, as the second movement of a symphony.
  • The Maker (1960) — The book Borges wrote when he was already going blind. It is a collection of poems, short prose pieces, miniatures, parables, and dreams. It is Borges' most personal book: "A man sets himself the task of drawing the world... but shortly before dying he discovers that this patient labyrinth of lines traces the image of his own face." That story ("The Maker") is the book in miniature. The Maker is Borges looking at himself from blindness, knowing he will die, and deciding that his complete work is a self-portrait. It is not the best entry point, but it is the book that best reveals the man behind the myth. Read it when you want to understand not Borges' ideas, but Borges.
  • Other Inquisitions (1952) — His most important book of essays. Not fictions or poems: essays on authors, ideas, philosophical and literary problems. Contains "Circular Time," "Pascal's Sphere," "Kafka and His Precursors," "The Mirror of Enigmas," and "The Analytical Language of John Wilkins." Here Borges does not tell stories — he thinks out loud about the stories of others. It is the book that shows how his mind works when it is not disguised as a narrator. If Ficciones and The Aleph are his stories, Other Inquisitions is the factory where those stories were made. Read it when you want to understand the Borges method: how he reads, how he steals, how he connects, how he creates from what others have written.
  • A Universal History of Infamy (1935) — His first recognized work of fiction (before that he had only published poems and essays). They are apocryphal biographies of infamous characters: the Shanghai assassin, the widow Ching (Chinese pirate), Billy the Kid, the impostor Tom Castro. Borges invents or distorts as much as he documents. It is the book where Borges discovers his narrative voice: spare, erudite, ironic, full of false details that seem real and real ones that seem false. It is the most accessible of all: the stories are short, direct, full of action and violence. If Ficciones seems too dense, start here.
  • The Book of Imaginary Beings (1967, with Margarita Guerrero) — A bestiary of fantastic creatures from all traditions (dragons, unicorns, the basilisk, the Phoenix, but also creatures invented by Borges). It is neither narrative nor essay — it is a taxonomy of wonder. Each entry is a small marvel of erudition and imagination. The entire book is an example of what Borges does best: creating order where there is none, and showing that all order is a fiction. Read it as a bedside book: open to a random page each night.

Connects with:

  • Deutsch (parallel universes) — The most direct and surprising connection. Borges imagined branching time ("The Garden of Forking Paths") in 1941, decades before Deutsch formalized the many-worlds interpretation in quantum computing. Ts'ui Pên's story, which describes a novel where the same event occurs in every possible way simultaneously, is a perfect literary description of the multiverse. But the connection goes deeper: both share the idea that knowledge does not accumulate — it branches. Borges says it in fiction; Deutsch demonstrates it in physics. Deutsch would say Borges was not doing literature — he was anticipating a deep truth about the structure of reality, using narrative as an epistemological vehicle. Borges is Deutsch before Deutsch, but in stories.
  • Taleb (the rare, what does not fit into categories) — Borges is the literary black swan. In "The Analytical Language of John Wilkins," Borges finds a Chinese classification of animals that is absurd — and shows that all classification is arbitrary, that the categories we use to understand the world are fictions. Taleb takes this intuition and applies it to economics and risk management: the black swan is what does not fit into existing categories, what breaks the classification. Both understand that the greatest danger is not the unknown — it is believing that our categories cover everything known. Borges says it from literary epistemology; Taleb from risk epistemology. Borges is the Taleb of ideas; Taleb is the Borges of markets. The connection between the Aleph (a point that contains everything) and Taleb's asymmetry (a small bet that can contain enormous returns) is profound: both seek the point of convergence where the small contains the infinite.
  • Watts (time as illusion) — Borges and Watts share the same central intuition: linear time is a convenient illusion, not a description of reality. The Borges of circular time (Nietzsche, the Eternal Return, "The Immortal") resonates with the Watts of the eternal present (the now as the only door to reality). Watts says it from Zen Buddhism and Taoism; Borges from literature and metaphysics. Both arrive at the same place: time is not an arrow, it is an ocean. But Borges adds a layer Watts does not have: anguish. For Watts, the dissolution of time is liberation; for Borges, it is vertigo. The difference is one of temperament, not philosophy.
  • Krishnamurti (the self as construction) — "Borges and I" could be a dialogue between Borges and Krishnamurti. Both hold that the self is a construction, a necessary fiction we mistake for reality. Krishnamurti says "the observer is the observed"; Borges says "I am not sure which of the two is writing this page." But there is a crucial difference: Krishnamurti seeks to dissolve the self through awareness; Borges knows the self is a fiction but does not want to dissolve it — he uses it as raw material for art. Krishnamurti is the mystic who wants to escape the labyrinth; Borges is the writer who wants to build more beautiful labyrinths.
  • Cabral (philosophy from the edge) — Borges and Cabral share the condition of thinkers who write from the margins of the canon. Borges from Buenos Aires, Cabral from northeastern Brazil. Both take the European tradition and twist it from their periphery, creating something the central tradition cannot produce. Borges reads universal literature as if it were Argentine; Cabral reads universal poetry as if it were from the Northeast. Both use the local as a window to the universal — not as an identity affirmation but as an epistemological starting point. There is no "Latin American philosophy" in Borges or Cabral — there is philosophy made from Latin America, which is different. Marginality is not a limitation: it is a point of view.
  • Feynman (no self-deception) — Borges resembles Feynman in nothing — style, temperament, or method. But they share a deep epistemic principle: radical honesty about what is not known. Feynman says "don't fool yourself"; Borges says "I don't know if history has meaning; I know it is secret." Both refuse to fill the gaps with comfortable narratives. Feynman rejects authority that pretends to know; Borges rejects literature that pretends to explain. Borges does not give answers — he gives questions in the form of stories. That is more honest than giving false answers. Feynman did not write stories, but if he had, they would have been like Borges': exact, relentless, beautiful, and deeply unsatisfying for anyone seeking certainties.
  • Popper (fallible knowledge) — Borges and Popper are contemporaries (Popper was born in 1902, Borges in 1899) and share a vision of knowledge as a fallible enterprise. "The Library of Babel" is Popper's epistemology told as a nightmare: there are too many books, most are garbage, and there is no infallible method to distinguish the true from the false. The difference: Popper believes the scientific method (conjecture and refutation) allows us to navigate the chaos; Borges believes the chaos is insurmountable and the best we can do is acknowledge it. Popper is optimistic about knowledge; Borges is tragic. But both reject induction, authority, and dogma. Borges is Popper without the hope.
  • Munger (via negativa) — Borges never wrote about investing, but his concept of "the library and selection" (not reading more, reading better) is pure via negativa. Munger says "investing is knowing what not to do"; Borges says "the infinite library is the problem, not the solution." Both understand that an excess of options is not freedom — it is paralysis. Borges anticipates the paradox of choice (which Schwartz would formalize decades later) from literature. But Munger has a practical answer (know what you don't know, stay within your circle of competence); Borges only has the question: "how do you choose a book in an infinite library?" The investor who reads Borges does not find answers — finds a more accurate description of his problem.
  • Graham (essays as thinking) — Graham and Borges are essayists who write to discover what they think. Both use the essay as a form of thought, not as an exposition of already-formed ideas. But Graham is digital, modern, pragmatic; Borges is classic, timeless, metaphysical. Graham writes about startups, programming, and writing; Borges writes about infinity, time, and identity. But in method — starting with a question, exploring without a plan, arriving at an unexpected place — they are identical. Graham has said Borges is one of his models as an essayist. It is no coincidence.

Expanded Pocket Quotes/Ideas:

  • "The original is unfaithful to the translation" — The phrase summarizes "Pierre Menard, Author of the Quixote" in seven words. The original (Cervantes) is "unfaithful" to its translation (Menard) because the modern reader reads the Quixote through all the literary history that came after — Cervantes did not know his book would be read in the light of Kafka, Joyce, and Borges himself. The translation is richer than the original because time has added layers of meaning the author could not foresee. The lesson: no work is complete until it confronts a reader who rewrites it with their own time. There are no sacred texts. No works are immune to reinterpretation.
  • "I don't know if history has meaning; I know it is secret" — Borges neither affirms nor denies that the universe has meaning. He affirms something more subtle: if it does, it is not given to us to know. History is not an open book — it is a closed book with a key we do not possess. This phrase is a warning against two errors: that of the one who believes history has clear meaning (progress, destiny, providence) and that of the one who believes it has no meaning at all (nihilism). Borges takes a third path: meaning exists but is secret, inaccessible, perhaps unsayable. The thinker's work is not to discover the secret — it is to honor it with the suspicion that it exists. Applied to investing: not knowing where the market is going is not a failure — it is an accurate description of reality. The one who claims to know where the market is going commits the first error; the one who says the market is random and there is nothing to understand commits the second. Borges gives you a third position: there is structure, but you do not know it.
  • "Death is a custom that everyone ends up acquiring" — Borges strips death of its drama and presents it as a habit. It is not a tragic event — it is a practice, a tradition, something everyone eventually does. The irony is in the word "acquiring": as if death were a custom one chooses, like smoking or reading the newspaper. This ironic distance is Borges' defense mechanism against the horror of mortality: if you tell it as a custom, it hurts less. The lesson is not about death — it is about how language can transform experience. The way you name something changes how you live it. It is a reminder that the narrative frame you choose for an event (a loss, a failure, a crisis) is not neutral: it determines your experience of that event.
  • "Paradise would be a library" — Borges' most quoted phrase, but almost always misinterpreted. He does not say paradise is like a library — he says it would be a library. It is a definition of paradise, not a comparison. Borges is saying that his personal heaven is an infinite library (the one of Babel? Another? The same?). But the Library of Babel is a prison — so is Borges' paradise a prison of books? Yes, exactly. For Borges, paradise is not freedom — it is the infinite possibility of reading. But that same infinite possibility is a condemnation because you can never read everything. Borges' paradise is tragic by definition. The phrase conceals a paradox: the Borgesian heaven is the hell of infinite choice. The lesson: be careful what you wish for, because your paradise may be a labyrinth.
  • "One man is all men" — From "The Immortal." The immortal has lived so many lives that he has been all men: he has been Homer and he has been no one, he has been general and slave, he has been everyone and no one. This phrase condenses the paradox of identity: if you live long enough, there is no "you" — there is a sequence of people who occupy your body. Borges is close to Buddhism: there is no permanent self, only a stream of consciousness that constantly changes. Applied to practical life: do not cling to one version of yourself. You are who you are today, but tomorrow you can be someone else. Fixed identities are useful fictions, not realities.
  • "Immortality is one of the worst pieces of news a man can receive" — The flip side of the previous phrase. If one is all men and lives forever, life loses its urgency. There is no hurry, no scarcity, no meaning. Borges intuits what modern philosophy has confirmed: mortality is not a design flaw — it is the condition of possibility for meaning. Things matter because they end. Love matters because it ends. Life matters because there is death. An immortal has no incentive to do anything — everything can wait, because there is infinite time. The lesson: finitude is not a curse, it is the engine of everything worthwhile.
  • "Mirrors and copulation are abominable because they multiply the number of men" — One of Borges' most cryptic phrases, from "Tlön, Uqbar, Orbis Tertius." Copulation (sex) multiplies bodies; mirrors multiply images. Both create copies, and for Borges the copy is always threatening because it suggests the original is not unique. If the world can be reflected or reproduced, then perhaps the world itself is a copy. The phrase conceals a metaphysical terror: if there are mirrors and sex, the world replicates uncontrollably, and what we believe is unique turns out to be just one more. Applied to startups: is your advantage replicable or unique? If it is replicable (a mirror, a copulation), it will not last.
  • "There is no intellectual exercise that is not useless" — Final line of "The Library of Babel." After describing the infinite library where every possible book exists, Borges concludes that all thought is futile because anything you can think of is already written in some book in the library — and was probably already thought by someone else. It is the most elegant way of saying "there are no original ideas." But the phrase has a trap: if all intellectual exercise is useless, then this phrase is too, and perhaps it isn't. Borges contradicts himself on purpose to show you that contradiction is the only way out of the absurd.
  • "Time is the substance I am made of. Time is a river that carries me away, but I am the river" — From "A New Refutation of Time." Borges tries to refute the existence of time, but in doing so discovers that he himself is made of time. He cannot refute time because time has constituted him. It is the ontological argument for time: I cannot deny it without affirming it, because I am its product. The phrase also contains the paradox of the river: I am the river that carries me — I am the flow and I am what flows. It is the most precise definition of identity as process, not as substance. I do not "have" time — I am time.
  • "What god behind god the plot begins / of dust and time and dream and agony?" — Final verses of "The Golem." Borges asks who created the creator. If man creates a Golem (an artificial being), who created man? And if that creator was created by another, where does the chain end? The question is the infinite regress of creation: there is never a first creator, always a god behind god. It is the same paradox as the Library of Babel (there is no fundamental book) and causality (there is no first cause). Borges' answer is not theological — it is aesthetic: the chain has no end, and the beauty lies in accepting the infinite, not in finding its origin.

Actionable Concepts from Borges

Concept 1: Labyrinth of Infinity

What it is: In Borges, infinity is concrete, oppressive, right here. The Library is infinite but the books have 410 pages. The Aleph is in the basement of a house in Buenos Aires. Infinity manifests in finite objects. The paradox: that which has no limits is contained in that which does.

When to use it: When a problem seems to have no solution. Instead of freezing, ask: what is the structure that gives form to this infinity?

Concrete example: You are evaluating 20 startups. The information is infinite. Define the coordinates of your library (ticket size, stage, industry) and accept that the rest of the universe exists but is not on your shelf.

How to apply it: Identify the finite variable within the infinite problem.

Antithesis: When the solution requires exhaustiveness (surgery, balance sheets, structural calculations).

Concept 2: Fiction as a Tool

What it is: For Borges, the best way to communicate a complex truth is not to demonstrate it — it is to present it as a story.

When to use it: When you need to communicate something the audience cannot grasp analytically.

Concrete example: Presenting a strategy change to a board. The numbers alone do not close the argument. Tell the story of two startups — one that did what everyone else does, another that did what no one understood.

How to apply it: Find the minimal story that contains your idea. Character, conflict, resolution.

Antithesis: Not useful for precise instructions or raw data.

Concept 3: The Original and the Copy

What it is: Pierre Menard writes the Quixote anew in the 20th century — being identical, it is richer than Cervantes'. There is no original work, only original readings.

When to use it: When you are paralyzed searching for the perfect or original solution.

Concrete example: You want to start a newsletter, but there are already a thousand. Your perspective — your bias, your judgment — makes it radically different.

How to apply it: Ask "is this authentic?" instead of "is this original?"

Antithesis: Does not apply to intellectual property.

Concept 4: The Mental Aleph

What it is: A point from which everything can be seen. Find, for any problem, the point of convergence.

When to use it: When you have too many variables and do not know where to start.

Concrete example: Evaluating a company. The Aleph could be the founder, the market timing, or a traction metric. Find THAT.

How to apply it: "If I could understand only ONE thing, what would it be?"

Antithesis: Not everything has a point of convergence.

Concept 5: Borges and I

What it is: Separate the "public self" from the "intimate self." The role is not you.

When to use it: When ego works against you, when you are too identified with your role.

Concrete example: An investment that did not work out. Ask: "Is this about me or about my role?"

How to apply it: Distance allows analysis without the noise of shame.

Antithesis: Not an excuse to evade responsibility.

Concept 6: The Library and Selection

What it is: The Library of Babel has every book — but that is the problem. Knowing what not to read is the true knowledge.

When to use it: When facing information overload. When you are in a loop of seeking more data before deciding.

Concrete example: You have been researching a startup for 3 hours. What you need to know is in the first 20 minutes.

How to apply it: Ask "will this change my decision?" If not, let it go.

Antithesis: Does not apply when learning a new subject.

How to Reinforce Borges

Weekly routine (15 min):

  1. Choose a story from Ficciones or The Aleph at random — do not choose it, let chance decide (like opening the library without knowing what to look for)
  2. Read it through once without taking notes. Let the story inhabit you for 24 hours without forced analysis.
  3. The next day, re-read it with a notebook. Note down: (a) the central idea in one sentence, (b) a connection to your life or work this week, (c) a question the story leaves you with.
  4. Ask yourself: "Where do I see this in my life today?" Do not force the connection — if there is none, that's fine. The story stays in the background.

Deepening routine (monthly):

  • Re-read ONE Borges story you have read before. Re-reading is the Borges method: the same text is another text when you read it again. Ask: "What do I see now that I did not see the first time?"
  • Read an essay from Other Inquisitions (not fiction, not a story — the Borges who thinks about what others wrote).
  • Listen to or read an interview with Borges. Watching him speak (or reading a transcript) gives you a dimension the written text does not: his voice, his pauses, his dry humor.

Practical exercise:

The next time you have to communicate something complex (an investment thesis, a team strategy, a difficult idea), write it as a 3-paragraph story. Rules:

  1. It must have a character facing a problem (not an abstract concept)
  2. It must have a conflict (what opposes the solution)
  3. It must have a resolution that contains the idea (it does not explain it — it shows it)

If you cannot write the story, you do not understand the idea well enough to communicate it. Borges is not decoration: it is a test of understanding. What cannot be told as a story is not truly understood.

Do not accumulate:

  • Borges is not read quickly. Do not try to read everything. One story per week, well digested, is worth more than all of The Aleph read in one night. Borges is for ruminating, not for gulping.
  • The danger of Borges is staying on the erudite surface (quoting his phrases, naming his stories) without doing the work of applying his ideas. Borges is not intellectual ornamentation — it is a toolkit of epistemological tools. Use it, do not display it.
  • Borges does not give answers: he gives questions in the form of stories. If you seek certainties, do not read him. If you seek better questions, there is no better companion.
  • The temptation of Borges is the labyrinth for its own sake: losing yourself in erudition, in references, in connections. The goal is not to know more — it is to see better. Use Borges to clean your gaze, not to fill your head.

Chapter 2Richard Feynman

Life:

Richard Phillips Feynman was born on May 11, 1918, in Far Rockaway, Queens, New York, into a lower-middle-class Jewish family. His father, Melville, was a military uniform salesman with an uncommon obsession: teaching his son to think, not to memorize. When Richard was a child, Melville showed him pieces of ceramic and explained that the patterns were not "flowers" but "wave interference in the glaze." He taught him that the name of a thing is not the same as the thing — that the bird is called "bird" in English, "oiseau" in French, "uccello" in Italian, but knowing those names tells you nothing about how it flies. This distinction between the map and the territory — between the label and the phenomenon — etched itself into Feynman as a first principle he never abandoned. His mother, Lucille, a housewife with a sharp sense of humor, balanced the father's scientific rigor with an equally important lesson: life is not only about thinking, it is also about laughing. Feynman learned both things, and his personality was an improbable synthesis of intellectual discipline and playful mischief.

He studied at MIT and then at Princeton, where he earned his doctorate in 1942. His thesis — a new approach to quantum mechanics based on the principle of least action — already bore the hallmark of his genius: he did not just solve problems, he invented new ways of thinking about them. At 25, he was recruited for the Manhattan Project at Los Alamos, where he worked alongside the giants of 20th-century physics: Oppenheimer, Bohr, Fermi, Bethe. There, Feynman not only made crucial theoretical contributions (he led the theoretical computation division that calculated the hydrodynamics of the bomb's implosion) but also earned a legendary reputation for two things: his ability to solve any technical problem faster than anyone else, and his total contempt for bureaucracy, hierarchy, and academic pomposity. He entertained himself by cracking safes using combinations he stole through his ability to observe patterns in the fingers of security officers. He played the bongos at Los Alamos parties. And he wrote letters to his wife Arline — who died of tuberculosis while he worked on the bomb — that years later would break the heart of anyone who read them. The apparent contradiction (the irreverent genius who was also a profoundly sensitive man) was in fact the key to his person: Feynman felt everything intensely, and his intensity channeled itself as much into science as into art, love, and fun.

After the war, Feynman revolutionized quantum physics with his diagrams (now known as Feynman diagrams), which radically simplified calculations in quantum electrodynamics (QED) and earned him the Nobel Prize in 1965, shared with Sin-Itiro Tomonaga and Julian Schwinger. He spent most of his career at Caltech, where he not only researched but created his most enduring pedagogical legacy: The Feynman Lectures on Physics, a two-year course that is perhaps the best introduction to physics ever written, not because it covers everything, but because it teaches you to think like a physicist. In 1986, at 68, Feynman lived his last great public act: he was a member of the Rogers Commission investigating the Challenger disaster. His intervention — the famous O-ring demonstration, where he put a piece of rubber in a glass of ice water and broke it with pliers to show it lost elasticity at low temperatures — is the quintessence of his method: simple, visual, devastatingly effective. Hundreds of pages of technical reports achieved what a glass of ice water, a pair of pliers, and a piece of rubber did in seconds. He died of abdominal cancer in 1988, at 69. On his blackboard, the day before he died, he had written: "What I cannot create, I do not understand."

Core Ideas:

  1. Do not confuse the map with the territory — This is the lesson his father taught him with the ceramic pieces and that Feynman elevated to a fundamental epistemological principle. Equations, models, theories — all are maps. They describe reality, but they are not reality. The most common trap of the intellect is falling in love with the map and forgetting the territory. An economic model can be beautiful in its internal consistency, but if it does not predict the actual behavior of markets, it is useless. A physical theory can be extremely elegant, but if the experiment does not support it, it is wrong. Feynman insisted on this with an almost religious ferocity: nature is in charge. No matter how pretty your theory is, no matter how brilliant you feel — if the data say otherwise, the error is yours. This humility before the facts is the foundation of all reliable knowledge. In everyday life, the principle translates into a constant question: am I operating on reality or on my mental model of reality? Most of our mistakes come from confusing the two.
  1. Do not fool yourself (and you are the easiest person to fool) — In his Caltech commencement speech, Feynman said the phrase that perhaps best defines him: "The first principle is that you must not fool yourself — and you are the easiest person to fool." This is not a warning about external lies, but about internal lies, which are far more dangerous because we do not detect them. Self-deception operates in subtle ways: we round data to fit our hypothesis, we discard contradictory evidence as "noise," we seek confirmation of what we already believe, and we construct narratives that make us feel good rather than narratives that bring us closer to the truth. Feynman understood that the scientific method is not a set of laboratory procedures — it is an existential stance: the active and conscious will not to lie to yourself. That is why science works not because scientists are more honest than everyone else, but because the method is designed to catch self-deception: independent replication, peer review, public exposure of data. In your personal and professional life, the corollary is brutal: when everything points in the same direction, when you are completely sure, when you find no crack in your reasoning — that is exactly when you should be most suspicious.
  1. If you cannot explain it simply, you do not understand it well enough — This phrase has become a cliché of startup culture and popular science, but Feynman gave it an epistemological weight that is rarely acknowledged. It is not pedagogical advice; it is a test of deep understanding. Complexity can be a refuge: when we use technical jargon, complicated equations, or convoluted arguments, we are often hiding — from ourselves and from others — that we do not really understand. Feynman could explain quantum electrodynamics (perhaps the most counterintuitive theory in physics) to first-year students because he really understood it. He did not understand it "for physicists" — he understood it. And to truly understand something means being able to translate it into simple language, find the analogy that reveals the structure, identify the essential core without technical ornament. If you cannot explain a concept to your grandmother or a twelve-year-old, the question is not "how do I explain it?" — the question is "what do I not understand?" Complexity is not a sign of depth. Sometimes depth is simple; sometimes complexity is confusion in disguise.
  1. Uncertainty is not a problem, it is a property — Feynman lived in the discomfort of quantum mechanics, a theory that describes a world where particles do not have definite positions until they are measured, where causality is probabilistic, where the observer affects the observed. For many of his contemporaries (Einstein among them), this was a temporary flaw: "God does not play dice." For Feynman, uncertainty was not an error to be eliminated — it was a fundamental feature of reality. "I don't know what's happening, but I know what it looks like," he would say. This attitude is profoundly liberating: the world is uncertain, but that does not mean we cannot navigate it. It means we have to abandon the illusion of control and learn to make decisions with incomplete information. Uncertainty is not an excuse not to decide — it is another piece of data. In investing, it means you do not ask for a single number of expected return, you ask for a range. In management, it means you do not pretend to know the future, but you design systems that work under multiple possible futures. Intellectual maturity is not eliminating uncertainty, it is learning to live with it.
  1. The pleasure of discovery as a driving force — Feynman did not do science out of ambition, duty, or recognition. He did it because it was fun. This is one of his most misunderstood lessons: people think curiosity is an attitude, when in fact it is a source of energy that never runs out. Feynman worked on physics problems because he could not help it: there was something he did not understand and that bothered him, intrigued him, fascinated him. The pleasure of discovery — the moment when something that made no sense suddenly clicks — was his fuel. In his book Surely You're Joking, Mr. Feynman!, he tells how he learned to draw, play the bongos, crack safes, decipher Mayan hieroglyphs, and diagnose mental illness — all out of pure curiosity, not utility. The practical lesson is powerful: when a project feels like an obligation, you are losing energy. When you find the curious angle, the detail that intrigues you, the question you genuinely want to answer — there you have unlimited energy. It is not that work should always be fun. It is that genuine curiosity is more powerful than any other form of motivation.
  1. Do not respect authority, respect evidence — The O-ring demonstration before the Rogers Commission was not just a memorable television moment. It was the embodiment of one of Feynman's deepest principles: concrete physical evidence beats any institutional authority, any committee, any hundred-page report. NASA had an organizational culture that prioritized hierarchy over data. Low-level engineers knew the O-rings had problems with cold, but they were not listened to because they had no authority. Feynman had no formal power over NASA — he was an elderly physics professor with no rocket experience. But he had a glass of ice water, a piece of rubber, and a pair of pliers. And that, combined with his willingness to say "this is wrong and I can prove it," was worth more than any title or position. The lesson is not "do not respect expertise" — expertise tells you where to look. But evidence is the final arbiter. When someone says "it is so because I say so" or "because it has always been done this way" or "because the committee approved it," Feynman would answer: "Show me the data."
  1. Knowledge is not sacred — Feynman laughed at everything, even himself. His irreverence was not a personality quirk — it was a deliberate epistemological stance. Solemnity is the enemy of critical thinking because serious people tend to protect their ideas rather than test them. Feynman treated the deepest theories of physics with the same irreverence with which he played the bongos in a bar. For him, knowledge was not a temple to be protected, but a tool to be used, questioned, improved, and — when necessary — discarded. This attitude led him to say things like "Science is the belief in the ignorance of experts" and "I don't have to know the answer. I'm not afraid to say I don't know something." The combination is lethal: not being afraid not to know, and not having reverence for those who claim to know. Knowledge is not sacred — it is provisional, fallible, and always open to revision. Laughing at it (and at oneself) is a way of remembering that.
  1. Think in diagrams (Feynman diagrams as cognitive technology) — Feynman revolutionized quantum electrodynamics not by solving equations faster, but by inventing a new way to represent problems: Feynman diagrams. Instead of pages of impossible integrals, Feynman drew lines and vertices representing particles interacting. Each diagram was a visual story of what happened in a quantum interaction. The genius was not only that it simplified calculations — it was that it changed the way of thinking about the problem. The act of drawing is not illustration: it is thinking. When you draw a problem, you force your brain to identify the essential parts, the relationships, the causality, the loops. Feynman diagrams are a perfect example of how a good representation is more valuable than a better algorithm. In practice, this means: when you face a complex problem, do not start doing calculations or writing text. Grab a sheet of paper and draw. Boxes and arrows. If you cannot draw it, you did not understand it. And if you can draw it, you probably already know how to solve it — or at least you know what to ask.

Key Works:

  • Surely You're Joking, Mr. Feynman! (1985) — The funniest autobiography ever written by a scientist. It is not a physics book: it is a collection of anecdotes where Feynman tells his life: how he learned to play the bongos in nightclubs, how he cracked safe combinations at Los Alamos, how he fooled psychiatrists with a hypnosis experiment, how he met his third wife in Geneva. But behind every joke there is a serious lesson: unfiltered curiosity, irreverence as method, not taking yourself too seriously. It is the book that best captures who Feynman was as a person. Start here.
  • The Feynman Lectures on Physics (1964, 3 volumes) — Feynman's most important pedagogical legacy. Originally a two-year course for Caltech students, it became the most influential physics book of the 20th century. What makes it unique is not the content (which covers mechanics, electromagnetism, quantum theory, thermodynamics), but how he explains it: Feynman does not teach physics, he teaches how to think like a physicist. Each chapter is a masterclass in how to approach an unknown problem, how to simplify without losing depth, how to find the intuition behind the equation. It is not a reference book: it is a training book.
  • Six Easy Pieces (1994) — A selection of six chapters from the Lectures designed for the non-specialist reader. Covers: atoms, fundamental physics, the relationship between physics and other sciences, conservation of energy, gravitational theory, and quantum behavior. It is the perfect entry point if the full Lectures feel overwhelming. In fewer than 200 pages, Feynman gives you the essence of his way of thinking.
  • The Character of Physical Law (1965) — A series of seven public lectures Feynman gave at Cornell University, broadcast by the BBC. It is the shortest and most accessible book of his serious work. Here Feynman does not teach equations: he teaches the philosophy of science, the nature of physical laws, the relationship between mathematics and reality, the role of symmetry, and the distinction between past and future. It is the book you would recommend to someone who wants to understand how a physicist thinks, not what he knows. Absolute gem.
  • What Do You Care What Other People Think? (1988) — The second part of his autobiography, published posthumously. Includes two very different sections: anecdotes from his personal life (his relationship with his first wife Arline, his father) and a detailed account of his involvement in the Challenger investigation. The first half is emotional and personal; the second is a case study of how to apply Feynman's method to real-world problems. The combination is devastating: it makes you laugh and cry, and leaves you with a deeper understanding of what it means to think honestly.
  • QED: The Strange Theory of Light and Matter (1985) — The most accessible explanation of quantum electrodynamics ever written for the general public. Feynman breaks the theory down into simple rules (drawing arrows, summing probabilities, considering all possible paths) and manages to make something that should be incomprehensible feel almost obvious. You do not need advanced mathematics — you need attention and an open mind.

Connects with:

  • Karl Popper (practical falsificationism) — Popper said a theory is scientific only if it can be falsified. Feynman lived that idea before he knew Popper existed. It is not that Feynman consciously applied Popperian falsificationism — it is that he had a natural instinct to seek what refuted his ideas rather than what confirmed them. Popper formalized epistemologically what Feynman practiced instinctively in the lab: science advances not by verifying theories, but by trying to break them. Feynman put it best: "I don't have to know the answer. I'm not afraid to say I don't know something." That willingness not to know — to actively seek refutation — is falsificationism in its purest form. The difference is that Popper wrote books; Feynman cracked safes and broke O-rings.
  • Nassim Nicholas Taleb (anti-institutional science, no self-deception) — Taleb is Feynman applied to markets and society. Both despise academic pomp, empty jargon, authority without evidence. Feynman says "do not fool yourself"; Taleb says "and especially not when money is involved." Taleb takes Feynman's scientific skepticism and turns it into a risk management system. Where Feynman used the scientific method to avoid self-deception, Taleb uses it to avoid financial ruin. Both share the same contempt for experts who speak without skin in the game, for economists who model without real data, for bureaucrats who decide without consequences. Feynman broke an O-ring with ice water; Taleb calls "fragilistas" those who ignore black swans. Same instinct, same enemy.
  • Charlie Munger (mental models, psychology of error) — Munger says the best way to think well is not to be right, but to avoid being stupid. Feynman said his superpower was not intelligence but not fooling himself. The connection is deep: both understood that the main obstacle to good judgment is not lack of information, but the psychology of error. Munger catalogs biases (perverse incentives, confirmation bias, overconfidence); Feynman lived them and proposed an antidote: methodical doubt as a habit. Munger from investing, Feynman from physics — but the diagnosis is identical. The antidote, too: ask yourself "what would have to happen for this to be a mistake?" instead of "how do I prove this works?"
  • Eliezer Yudkowsky (applied rationality) — Yudkowsky is the direct heir of Feynman in the field of rationality. He took Feynman's warning ("do not fool yourself") and built a full engineering of rationality: cognitive biases, confidence calibration, Bayesian epistemology. Yudkowsky is what happens when you read Feynman and say "this should apply to everything, not just physics." Both share the idea that intellectual honesty is not an abstract moral value but a technical skill that can be trained. And both are controversial for their style: direct, irreverent, with no patience for institutional stupidity. Feynman at Caltech, Yudkowsky on LessWrong — same spirit, different century.
  • Paul Graham (clarity as a measure of understanding) — Graham says "if you cannot say it in a simple sentence, you do not understand it." Feynman says "if you cannot explain it to a first-year student, you do not understand it." It is exactly the same principle. But Graham applies it to writing startups, essays, and business ideas, while Feynman applied it to quantum mechanics. Both use clarity as a test of real understanding. And both understand that unnecessary complexity is not depth — it is noise. Graham builds startups by simplifying business models; Feynman builds theories by simplifying mathematical representations. Same intuition, different domains.
  • Daniel Kahneman (psychology of self-deception) — Kahneman explains the mechanism by which it is so hard not to fool yourself. System 1 is the perfect self-deception machine: fast, intuitive, full of biases. System 2 is lazy and goes along. Feynman diagnosed the problem ("you are the easiest person to fool") decades before Kahneman won the Nobel for explaining the mechanics. Feynman gave the moral and epistemological warning; Kahneman gave the psychological explanation. Together they form a complete system: Feynman says "do not fool yourself," Kahneman says "here is why it is inevitable and here is how to mitigate it." If you read Feynman for the attitude and Kahneman for the tool, you have a serious defense against your own brain.
  • Peter Thiel (thinking from zero, going against the herd) — Thiel insists that intellectual honesty is rare and valuable, especially in investing. His favorite question — "what is true that few people accept?" — is pure Feynman. Both share a contempt for intellectual conformism, for authority without evidence, for ideas accepted only because they are accepted. But Thiel is more constructive (inventing the future) and Feynman more critical (not fooling yourself about the present). Thiel seeks secrets no one else sees; Feynman seeks errors everyone prefers to ignore. The bond is intellectual honesty as the supreme value, above social belonging or mental comfort.
  • David Deutsch (scientific method as attitude) — Deutsch extends Feynman's attitude — no self-deception, clarity as measure, the scientific method as the only reliable form of knowledge — into a complete philosophy of human knowledge. Deutsch takes Feynman's practice and turns it into theory: the advancement of knowledge as the engine of all progress, explanation as the fundamental unit, fallibilism as the foundation of civilization. Feynman is practical, Deutsch is theoretical, but both share the same radical intellectual honesty. Feynman says "I don't know what's happening, but I know what it looks like"; Deutsch says "objective knowledge exists and we can create it without limit." Feynman is the demonstration that it works; Deutsch is the explanation of why it works.

Expanded Pocket Quotes/Ideas:

  • "The first principle is that you must not fool yourself — and you are the easiest person to fool" — The most important phrase of Feynman, and perhaps the most important of this entire Pantheon. It is not about objective truth, it is about subjective honesty. The most dangerous person for your knowledge is not the one who lies to you — it is yourself, rounding data, ignoring evidence, seeking confirmation. The scientific method is, above all, a technology to fight your own self-deception. Use it.
  • "I don't know what's happening, but I know what it looks like" — One of the most honest epistemological statements ever made by a scientist. Feynman admits he does not understand the ultimate nature of quantum reality, but he knows how to describe its behavior accurately. It is intellectual maturity in one sentence: you can operate effectively in the world without having all the ultimate answers. You do not need to know why something works to use it well. But you need to be honest about what you do not know.
  • "What do you care what other people think?" — The title of his second autobiographical book is also his life attitude. Feynman did not ask permission to be curious, to be wrong, to be ridiculous, to play the bongos in a nightclub while solving quantum equations in his head. The question is a reminder that social approval is a silent enemy of independent thinking. It is not about being antisocial — it is about not letting the fear of others' judgment limit your curiosity.
  • "If you think you understand quantum mechanics, you don't understand quantum mechanics" — Feynman's favorite paradox. Quantum mechanics is so counterintuitive that the feeling of "understanding it" is probably an illusion. The phrase applies beyond physics: in any complex field, excessive confidence is a sign that you are not seeing the depth. If you think you already understand a complex problem, you probably do not. Doubt is not weakness — it is the only rational stance before real complexity.
  • "I don't have to know the answer. I'm not afraid to say I don't know something." — Feynman considered this willingness to say "I don't know" his greatest intellectual strength. In a culture that rewards confidence and punishes uncertainty, saying "I don't know" is an act of epistemological courage. But it is also a smart strategy: if you admit you do not know, you open the door to learning. If you pretend to know, you close it. Ignorance is not shameful — it is the natural state of the learner.
  • "Science is the belief in the ignorance of experts" — One of the most provocative definitions of science. Feynman does not say experts know nothing — he says science is based on the recognition that no one knows enough, that there is always more to learn, that authority is not an argument. Science is not a repository of truths but a machine for producing productive ignorance: each answer opens ten new questions. An expert who forgets this is not an expert, but a dogmatist.
  • "Physics is like sex: sure, it gives no practical results, but that's why we do it" — Feynman at his most irreverent. The phrase captures his attitude toward science: it does not need utilitarian justification. Curiosity is an end in itself. Not everything worth doing has to be useful. The pleasure of understanding how the world works is reason enough. In a world obsessed with productivity, remembering that some things are done because they are interesting is almost subversive.
  • "What would I do if I weren't afraid?" — The question Feynman asked himself when facing difficult situations. It is not that he did not feel fear — he felt it, and deeply, like anyone. But he did not let fear decide for him. The question is not "how do I eliminate fear?" but "what would I do if fear weren't there?" That is true courage: acting despite fear, guided by curiosity and not by avoidance.
  • "What I cannot create, I do not understand" — The last sentence he wrote on his blackboard before dying. It is perhaps the most profound statement about the nature of understanding. You do not understand something until you can rebuild it from scratch. Reading, listening, memorizing — that is not understanding. Understanding is being able to create. In investing: you do not understand a business until you can model its cash flows. In programming: you do not understand a system until you can rewrite it. In life: you do not understand a decision until you can explain why you made it.
  • "There are a thousand ways not to understand quantum mechanics. But it doesn't matter — nature is that way." — The radical acceptance that the universe does not have to conform to our intuitions. Reality does not ask permission. It does not matter if a theory seems strange, counterintuitive, even offensive — if the data support it, reality is that way, and our job is to adapt our intuition, not to complain that nature is not as we would like it.

Actionable Concepts from Feynman

Concept 1: Self-deception as Enemy #1

What it is: The greatest obstacle to truth is not the external lie, but the internal one. We deceive ourselves by rounding data, discarding contradictory evidence, seeking confirmation.

When to use it: When everything points in the same direction. Be suspicious.

Concrete example: You are evaluating a startup. Everything fits. Feynman: "Tell me what's wrong. Show me the data that would make you doubt."

How to apply it: Write "What would have to happen for this to be a mistake?"

Antithesis: Not for total paralysis. For important, non-trivial decisions.

Concept 2: Explain as if to your mother

What it is: If you cannot explain it to a first-year student, you do not understand it.

When to use it: Presenting a thesis, teaching, writing.

Concrete example: Explaining deep tech to an LP: "Imagine a WhatsApp that works without internet on a mountain."

How to apply it: Write it in 280 characters. If it does not fit, you did not understand it.

Antithesis: With expert peers, technical precision is necessary.

Concept 3: Uncertainty as Data

What it is: Uncertainty is inherent, it is not eliminated with more data.

When to use it: When you are asked for certainties you cannot give.

Concrete example: LP asks for expected IRR. Answer with a range and critical variable, not a single number.

How to apply it: Always three scenarios: base, optimistic, pessimistic.

Antithesis: Not an excuse not to do the numbers.

Concept 4: Think in Diagrams

What it is: Feynman revolutionized physics by drawing interactions. Drawing is thinking.

When to use it: Problems with multiple actors and interactions.

Concrete example: Marketplace: draw buyers, sellers, platform. The arrows show dynamics a spreadsheet does not capture.

How to apply it: Boxes and arrows. If you cannot draw it, you did not understand it.

Antithesis: Not for linear problems.

Concept 5: The O-ring (Evidence > Authority)

What it is: Feynman vs. NASA: ice water + pliers + rubber demonstrated what reports could not prove.

When to use it: When hierarchy dominates the decision.

Concrete example: In an investment committee: "Give me the concrete metric. Show me the data."

How to apply it: "How do we know this? What is the demonstration?"

Antithesis: Not for dismissing anyone who knows more. Expertise tells you where to look.

Concept 6: The Pleasure of Discovery as a Driving Force

What it is: He did science because it was fun. Genuine curiosity is more powerful than ambition.

When to use it: When a project feels like an obligation.

Concrete example: Tedious deal sourcing. Turn it into a game: "find the weirdest startup possible this week."

How to apply it: Reframe as an experiment. Find the curious angle.

Antithesis: Not everything is fun-able. The trick is to find, within the tedious, the detail that intrigues.

How to Reinforce Feynman

Weekly routine (15 min):

  1. Choose ONE concept you "think" you understand well (it could be from investing, your work, everyday life)
  2. Explain it to an imaginary someone who knows nothing about the subject — as if to your grandmother or a 12-year-old
  3. Wherever you get stuck, wherever you use jargon, wherever you feel "this cannot be simplified further" — that is precisely what you do not understand
  4. Note that gap. That gap is your homework for the week: investigate it until you can explain it simply
  5. At the end of the week, re-read your explanation. If you still use jargon, you have not understood it yet

Deepening routine (monthly):

  • Re-read ONE chapter of The Feynman Lectures on Physics — not to learn physics, but to see how Feynman explains. Pay attention to the analogies, the questions he asks, how he simplifies without losing rigor. That is what matters.
  • Read a chapter of Surely You're Joking, Mr. Feynman! — not for entertainment (though it is), but to absorb his attitude: unfiltered curiosity, irreverence as method.
  • Watch some of his interviews or lectures on YouTube. Seeing him gesticulate, laugh, ask uncomfortable questions — that is understanding him live.

Practical exercise (next week):

Keep a "self-deception diary." Each day, identify a situation where you caught yourself rounding data, ignoring contradictory evidence, or seeking confirmation of what you already believed. Do not judge yourself — just record it. At the end of the week, read the diary. What you see will make you uncomfortable. That is progress: the discomfort of seeing yourself as you really are, not as you think you are.

Do not accumulate:

  • You do not need to read the entire Lectures to use Feynman. With Surely You're Joking + The Character of Physical Law you have the core of his attitude and method. The Lectures are for deepening, not for starting.
  • The danger with Feynman is adopting the pose of "the irreverent guy who challenges authority" without doing the hard work of really understanding things. Irreverence without rigor is just arrogance.
  • Feynman is not an anti-intellectual — he is anti-pomposity. The difference is subtle but crucial. It is not about despising expert knowledge, but about not confusing authority with truth.
  • The final Feynman test is not "does it sound good?" nor "is it fun?" — it is "does it work?" and "do the data support it?" Keep that standard, and Feynman will never fail you.

Chapter 3Nassim Nicholas Taleb

Life: Nassim Nicholas Taleb (1960–), essayist, statistician, and former Lebanese-American financial trader. Born in Amioun, Lebanon, into a Greek Orthodox family with roots in politics and academia — his grandfather was deputy prime minister and his father an oncologist. He grew up in a cosmopolitan, multilingual Lebanon, surrounded by French intellectuals, Levantine aristocrats, and the background noise of a civil war that erupted when he was 15. That experience — a society disintegrating overnight, where what was secure yesterday becomes impossible today — planted his lifelong obsession: the world is not predictable, stability is an illusion, and most of the important things that happen you did not see coming.

He studied finance at the Wharton School and then earned an MBA and a PhD in risk management from Paris-Dauphine University and the Sorbonne. But Taleb is not a classic academic — he is an academic forged in the real world. Before writing books, he worked as a trader on Wall Street for 20 years: he was an options trader at Bankers Trust, Credit Suisse, and Union Bank of Switzerland. His business was not buying and selling stocks — it was selling protection against extreme events. In other words: being on the opposite side of people who think they know what will happen. If history taught him anything, it is that markets systematically underestimate the probability of rare and extreme events. And he built his entire career (and fortune) around that intuition.

After September 11, 2001, and the 2008 financial crisis — two perfect black swans he had anticipated in his books — Taleb retired from trading and dedicated himself to writing and teaching. He is currently a professor at New York University (NYU Tandon) and the University of Massachusetts Amherst, but he does not teach normal classes: he gives seminars, writes incendiary essays, and has become one of the most influential and polarizing voices in risk theory. He is famous for his confrontational style: he calls neoclassical economists "fragilistas," those who ignore fat-tail theory "Mediocristanians," and has public feuds with Nobel laureates he considers fraudulent. But he is not a troll — he is a thinker who understands that uncomfortable truth needs to be said forcefully to break through the noise of consensus.

His work is organized in the Incerto: five volumes (Fooled by Randomness, The Black Swan, The Bed of Procrustes, Antifragile, Skin in the Game) that form a complete philosophical system on how to navigate a world we cannot predict but can understand at its limits. He is not just a critic of economics — he is a philosopher of risk, a practical epistemologist who wants us to stop pretending we know what we do not know.

Core Ideas:

  1. The Black Swan (rare, unpredictable events of great impact) — A Black Swan is an event that (a) is an extreme rarity, outside normal expectations, (b) carries a massive impact, and (c) after it occurs, people rationalize it as if it had been predictable (hindsight bias). World War I, 9/11, the 2008 crisis, the COVID-19 pandemic — all are black swans. The problem is not that they occur; the problem is that systems (social, economic, political) are designed as if they will not occur. Taleb does not say we can predict black swans — he says we can build systems that do not break when they arrive. The mistake is not not knowing they will happen; the mistake is acting as if they cannot happen.
  1. Antifragility (what benefits from disorder) — Taleb coined this term to describe something that does not exist in common language. There are fragile things (broken by shock), robust things (resist shock without breaking), and antifragile things (become stronger from shock). The immune system is antifragile: exposing yourself to a virus strengthens you. Muscles are antifragile: the stress of exercise damages them and they rebuild stronger. An ecosystem is antifragile: periodic fires renew the forest. Well-designed startups are antifragile: small failures teach lessons and the company strengthens. Antifragility is not resilience (returning to the same point) — it is improvement through stress. The goal is not to avoid chaos, it is to build systems that need it to grow.
  1. The problem of induction (the turkey who thinks the butcher is his friend) — Taleb takes from Hume and Popper the deepest problem of epistemology: we cannot infer the future from the past. The turkey is fed day after day by the butcher. Each day of feeding reinforces his belief that the butcher is good and the world is safe. Until the day before Thanksgiving, when the butcher cuts his throat. The turkey had 1,000 days of evidence in favor of his theory — and one single day destroyed it completely. The problem is not that he lacked data; it is that the kind of data he needed (the day of the slaughter) did not exist in his dataset. In investing, this translates to: a strategy that worked for 10 years can fail catastrophically in year 11 if conditions change. The past is not a reliable guide to the future when there is non-stationarity and fat tails.
  1. Confirmation bias and retrospective narrative — The human brain constructs stories after the fact to make sense of events, and those stories make us believe we knew what was going to happen. Taleb calls this "the narrative fallacy": the tendency to create a coherent story from random data, and then believe the story explains (and predicts) reality. Hindsight bias (the "I knew it all along") is a machine of self-deception: after an event, everything seems to have been inevitable and predictable. The black swan is not that it cannot be predicted — it is that afterward everyone believes it could have been. Most post-mortem analyses of failures are useful fictions, not accurate descriptions of what happened.
