When to use it: At any decision phase: evaluating a startup, choosing a market, defining a strategy, evaluating a founder. If you do not have an answer to Thiel's question, do not move forward. The question forces you to find a belief that is (a) true (b) important and (c) not obvious. Without that, you are operating on consensus — and in consensus there is no advantage.

Concrete example: You are evaluating an AI startup applied to healthcare. Most investors say "AI in healthcare is the future." That is not counterintuitive — it is what everyone says. Thiel's question: "what do we know about AI in healthcare that almost no one knows?" A possible answer: "hospitals will not pay for predictive AI because they have no incentives to prevent — they will pay for diagnostic AI because it reduces litigation." If that answer is true, it completely changes how you evaluate the startup: do not look at predictive accuracy, look at legal risk reduction. Your investment pipeline focuses on startups that speak the language of insurance, not preventive health.

How to apply it: For any investment thesis, write the answer to Thiel's question. If you cannot write something specific and falsifiable, the thesis is weak. If you can, use that answer as your main selection criterion — it is your differential advantage. Each year, update your answers: what was counterintuitive yesterday may be obvious today.

Antithesis: Does not apply when you need consensus to execute (operations, logistics, construction). The counterintuitive question is for strategic direction, not execution. Nor is it an excuse for cheap contrarianism: it is not being different for the sake of being different. It is finding genuine truths that others do not see.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Choose a deal, a sector, or a trend. Force yourself to write the answer to Thiel's question in one sentence. If you cannot, study more. If you can, ask yourself: "and if this is true, what should I do differently?"
  • Monthly: Review your previous answers. Are they still counterintuitive or have they become obvious? If they have become obvious, look for new ones.
  • Practical exercise: In your next investment meeting, before evaluating a deal, each person writes their answer to Thiel's question about the deal's sector. Compare. The discrepancies are more interesting than the agreements.

Concept 2: Monopoly in a Small Market First

What it is: Thiel inverts the intuition of "big market or die." He says: do not start in a big market — start in a small market where you can be the only player. Dominate it completely. Then expand. The reason: in a big, competitive market, you will spend all your time dealing with competitors, not building value. In a small market where you are the only one, you can build deep defenses. The big market is a trap for startups — it is for established companies. PayPal started with 200,000 Palm Pilot users (a tiny market). Facebook started with Harvard (12,000 students). Amazon started with books. Airbnb started with design conferences in San Francisco. All started small, dominated their niche, and then expanded.

When to use it: When evaluating a startup that says "our market is $10B." That is a red flag — they are thinking about market size before thinking about product and defense. Prefer the startup that says "our initial market is $50M but no one serves it well and we can be the only ones." It also applies when defining your own strategy: do not try to cover too much at the beginning. Find the niche where you can be #1.

Concrete example: A software startup for SME accounting. Market: tens of billions, but with established competitors (QuickBooks, Xero, FreshBooks). The Thiel approach: do not compete in "accounting software" — find a sub-market where you can be the only one: "accounting software for dental clinics in Latin America." It is small (~2,000 clinics, $5M market), but if you manage to dominate it, you have 100% market share. Then expand to clinics in general, then to healthcare, then to professional services. First local monopoly, then global market.

How to apply it: For any startup, define the smallest market where it can be #1. If you cannot answer "what market are we going to be monopolists in?" in one sentence, the startup has no defense. The size of the current market matters less than the ability to dominate it. A small dominated market is worth more than a large market where you are #5.

Antithesis: Does not work in markets that depend on massive network effects (platforms where value grows with the number of users) — there you have to scale fast because the winner takes all. But even in those cases, start small: Facebook started at Harvard.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Choose a well-known company. Identify what its initial market was (the one it dominated before expanding). Ask: "why that market and not another?"
  • Monthly: Review your investment pipeline. For each deal, identify the smallest market where it can be #1. If you cannot, reconsider the deal.
  • Practical exercise: Take a startup in your portfolio or pipeline. Define a smaller market (sub-niche) where it could be a monopolist. If that sub-niche does not exist, ask yourself if the startup should focus more.

Concept 3: Actively Seek Secrets

What it is: A secret is an important truth that few discover because few are looking. Modern culture (the "disenchantment") tells us that everything important has already been discovered. Thiel says that is false and paralyzing. Secrets exist everywhere: in technology (quantum computing will change cryptography in ways no one anticipates), in business (there are entire markets no one is serving), in human behavior (people want X but do not know how to express it or dare to ask for it). Finding a secret is not easy — it requires believing they exist, looking where others do not look, and having the courage to explore what seems strange or improbable.

When to use it: When the market feels saturated. When it seems like everything has been invented. When conventional wisdom says "that's already been solved" or "that doesn't work." Those are the signs that there are secrets to be discovered. If everyone believes the same thing about a market, it is very likely they are wrong or have stopped looking. Your job as an investor/founder is not to accept "everything has already been discovered" — and to actively seek what no one else is looking for.

Concrete example: In 2004, people said "social networks are already solved — we have MySpace, Friendster, LinkedIn." The secret Thiel saw in Facebook: "real (not anonymous) identity in social networks is more valuable than fake identity." It was not obvious in 2004. Most assumed anonymity was the key feature (it was what worked in forums and chats). Thiel bet that real identity was the secret — and won. Today it seems obvious, but in 2004 it was counterintuitive. That is a secret.

How to apply it: When evaluating a market, ask: "what is everyone assuming that could be false?" If you find an unexamined assumption (something everyone takes for granted without evidence), examine it. That could be where the secret lies. The answer is never obvious — if it were, it would not be a secret. The exercise of seeking secrets is not academic — it should be part of your weekly thinking routine.

Antithesis: Do not confuse secrets with conspiracies. A secret is a verifiable truth that few know. A conspiracy is an unverifiable narrative that explains the power of others as a plot. They are opposites. A secret gives you an investment advantage; a conspiracy gives you a story that makes you feel enlightened while keeping you paralyzed.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Identify one unexamined assumption in your industry or portfolio. Ask: "and if this were false? What would change?"
  • Monthly: Read something from ONE industry that is not yours (biotech, defense, energy, agriculture). The best ideas come from applying frameworks from one domain to another.
  • Practical exercise: Make a list of 5 "obvious truths" about investing right now. For each, write: "and if it's a lie?" It does not matter if the answer is uncomfortable — the exercise is training the muscle of finding secrets.

Concept 4: Definite Optimism as a Personal Strategy

What it is: It is not enough to be optimistic about the future. You must have a specific thesis about how it will be better and how you will contribute to that. Indefinite optimism ("technology will improve everything") does not produce action — it produces passive waiting. Definite optimism ("I am going to build a $50M fund focused on deep tech in LATAM") produces action, investment, and results. Thiel sees indefinite optimism as the disease of Silicon Valley: many people believe in "the future," few bet big on something specific. Definition is not limitation — it is direction. Definite optimism does not guarantee success, but it guarantees direction. And without direction, you do not get anywhere.

When to use it: When defining a strategy, personal or professional. When you find yourself saying "I want to be successful" without being able to define what that means. When your plan is vague ("invest in startups") without specifying which ones, when, how. Definite optimism forces you to get concrete: what exactly are you going to build? For whom? On what timeline? The answer does not have to be perfect — it has to be specific.

Concrete example: "I want to invest in startups" is indefinite optimism. "I am going to build a $30M fund focused on agricultural biotechnology startups in Argentina, with the thesis that climate change will force the adoption of drought-resistant crops in the next 5 years" is definite optimism. It directs your search, attracts like-minded LPs (who understand agriculture and climate), and gives you clear investment criteria. It does not matter if the thesis is perfect — what matters is that you have one. Without it, you are a reactive investor, not a creative one.

How to apply it: For any important plan, write a sentence that starts with "I am going to build X to achieve Y in Z time" and make it as specific as possible. If you cannot, you do not have definite optimism — you have a vague hope. Once you have the sentence, use it as a filter: every weekly decision should move you toward or away from that objective. If it does not move you closer, do not do it.

Antithesis: Do not confuse specificity with rigidity. Thiel's definite optimism includes course correction. The plan changes, but the direction does not. It is not a GPS that takes a wrong street and sends you to a different destination — it is a ship with a destination that adjusts sails to the wind. The initial specificity is a hypothesis, not a dogma.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Review your main plan. Ask: "what I did this week — does it move me toward my definite optimism or distract me?"
  • Monthly: Review and update your definite optimism sentence. Is it still correct? Does it need adjustment? But do not change it on a whim — only if the evidence shows your original thesis was incorrect.
  • Practical exercise: Write your personal (not professional) definite optimism sentence for the next 3 years. Share it with someone you trust. Have them ask: "why this specifically?" If you cannot defend it, reformulate it.

Concept 5: Power at the Vertex vs. Consensus

What it is: Thiel argues that successful startups in early stages need concentrated power. A startup is not a democracy — too many people deciding produces zero speed and death by consensus. Power at the vertex means that someone (usually the founder/CEO) has the final word and the final responsibility. It does not mean authoritarianism or abuse — it means clarity: who decides when there is disagreement. Without that clarity, the startup paralyzes itself in endless discussions or drifts without direction. As the company grows, power can be distributed more (you would not want the founder to decide on every feature in a company of 10,000 people). But at the beginning, concentration of power defines execution speed.

When to use it: When evaluating the dynamics of a founding team. If they have a "co-CEO" or a committee for every decision, it is a red flag. Thiel prefers clearly vertical founders (Zuckerberg, Musk, Bezos, Jobs) over collegiate founders (where no one has the final word). Also when building your own company or fund: define early who has the final word for each type of decision.

Concrete example: PayPal had Elon Musk as CEO (then Thiel replaced him). It was not a flat organization. Important decisions (what to do about fraud, how to position the product, which market to attack) were made by one person. That enabled speed at critical moments (when fraud threatened the company's existence, there was no time for committees). In contrast, many startups die from "death by consensus": trying to get everyone to agree, they lose market timing. Decision speed is a competitive asset.

How to apply it: When analyzing a startup, ask: "who makes the final decision if there is a disagreement among the founders?" If the answer is "the team" or "it depends" or "we vote," it is a warning sign. If the answer is a specific person (CTO, CEO, founder), it has structure. It does not matter if you do not like the answer — what matters is that it exists. Clarity of power is more important than the type of power.

Antithesis: It is not a universal recipe. In companies with senior professionals and high human capital (Google, Microsoft in certain eras, investment funds with multiple partners), distributed power works better. The power structure should match the type of business and the moment of the company. Also: concentrated power does not mean unilateral decisions without information — the leader must listen, but decides.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Observe the decisions in your team or organization. When there is disagreement, who decides? If there is no clarity, identify why and propose a structure.
  • Monthly: Review the startups in your pipeline. For each, identify the power structure. Those with clarity (one person decides) have an advantage over those without.
  • Practical exercise: Define for your own work (or fund, or project) who has the final word for each type of key decision. Write it down. If you cannot, you have a structural problem.

Concept 6: Distribution of Sales and Secrets

What it is: Thiel classifies businesses by their predominant sales method. There are complex sales (large contracts, long cycles, many stakeholders — e.g., Palantir selling to governments), personal sales (the founder sells one-on-one — e.g., early-stage startups), marketing sales (advertising, content, branding — e.g., Coca-Cola, Nike), and virality (the product sells itself because users pass it on — e.g., Facebook, WhatsApp, Slack). Each business model requires a specific sales method, and confusing them is fatal. But the concept goes further: secrets are also distributed in particular ways. Some secrets are public but not applied (everyone knows attention is on mobile, but few build truly for mobile). Others are known by a few and worth seeking. Understanding how knowledge (and sales power) is distributed in your industry gives you an advantage.

When to use it: When evaluating a startup's business model. The question is not only "is the product good?" — it is "how is it sold?" A startup with an excellent product but no clear sales method dies. Also when evaluating the investment thesis: what secret has the startup discovered about how to sell? If there is none, they are probably competing on cost or product — and both are fragile.

Concrete example: A B2B enterprise SaaS startup that sells at $10K/year but expects clients to come through content marketing. Thiel: that does not work. Enterprise clients do not buy from a blog post — they need a complex sales process (demo, trial, procurement, legal, security). The startup needs an enterprise sales team, not a blog. Conversely, a B2C app that expects to sell $10K/year to individual users with a sales team also does not work — it needs virality or mass marketing. Distribution determines the business model, not the other way around.

How to apply it: For any startup in your pipeline, ask: "how do they get customers?" and "does that method scale with the unit economics of the business?" If a startup sells at $100/contract, it cannot have a 6-month sales cycle with a team of 10 salespeople. If a startup sells at $1M/contract, the founder should be selling personally the first 10 clients. Distribution must match the price and type of customer.

Antithesis: There is no "best" sales method. The best method is the one that works for the product, the price, and the market. Virality is not better than complex sales — they are for different products. What does not work is the mismatch between product and distribution.

How to reinforce:

  • Weekly: Choose a startup and classify its sales method (complex, personal, marketing, viral). Ask: "is it the right method for the product and price?"
  • Monthly: Review your pipeline. For each startup, evaluate whether the sales method is realistic. If you find a mismatch, point it out.
  • Practical exercise: Take a business you know well. Map its sales distribution. Identify the secret (or lack of secret) in how they sell. If there is no secret, why does not the competition copy them?

How to Reinforce Thiel

Weekly routine (10 min):

  1. Choose a newsworthy company (a startup that appeared on TechCrunch, a deal you saw, a company that caught your attention)
  2. Apply Thiel's test: is it 0 to 1 or 1 to n?
  3. Ask: what secret did this company discover? If there is no secret, there is no defense
  4. Ask: what is the counterintuitive truth that sustains this company? If there is none, it is a copy
  5. Ask: does it have a monopoly in a small market or competition in a large market?

Deepening routine (monthly):

  • Re-read Zero to One once a year. It is short and each reading reveals new layers based on your experience as an investor.
  • Read the essay "The Straussian Moment" to understand his political thinking — it is controversial but expands his framework beyond startups.
  • Follow Founders Fund and their public investment theses. Thiel no longer writes as much, but his fund publishes its vision.
  • Read about the PayPal Mafia: how a group of people (Musk, Thiel, Levchin, Hoffman, Sacks, Rabois) built companies that changed the world. The collective pattern is more interesting than any individual.

Practical exercise: The next time you see a saturated market (food delivery, fintech, crypto, AI), try to find a "secret" — an unexamined assumption that the majority takes for granted. Write it down. Then ask: "if this is true, what business could be built?" It does not matter if you build it — the exercise of seeking secrets changes your bias from "everything has been discovered." Do this with a different market each month.

Do not accumulate:

  • You do not need to read all of Thiel's work to use him. Zero to One is sufficient as a foundation. The rest is deepening and context.
  • The danger with Thiel is adopting the pose of "I am the only one who understands" without having the substance. The counterintuitive question requires real work — it is not being different for the sake of being different. Contrarianism without study is pose, not advantage.
  • Thiel is more useful as a filter than as a recipe. His ideas are better for saying "this does not work" than for saying "this will work." Use him to prune your pipeline, not to build deals from scratch.
  • Do not confuse Thiel's critique of competition with an excuse not to do competitive analysis. His point is not to compete in crowded markets, not to ignore competitors. Know them, do not imitate them.
  • The political Thiel and the investor Thiel are different people. You can take the investment ideas (0 to 1, secrets, monopolies) without buying the political ones (critiques of democracy, transhumanism). Separate them mentally.

Chapter 6Karl Popper

Life: Karl Popper (1902–1994), Austrian-British philosopher. Born in Vienna into a Jewish family that had converted to Protestantism. He witnessed the rise of Nazism and of logical positivism (the Vienna Circle) — two dogmas that would mark his philosophy from the opposite side. He fled Austria in 1937, first to New Zealand and then to the United Kingdom. He was never accepted at Oxford or Cambridge; he taught at the London School of Economics, where he formed a school of thought without explicitly seeking one. His first book, The Logic of Scientific Discovery (1934, in German Logik der Forschung), went unnoticed at first but ended up being one of the most influential texts in 20th-century philosophy. Popper did not believe in induction, did not believe in verification, did not believe in certainty. He believed in one thing: that knowledge advances by eliminating errors. That idea — simple, radical, uncomfortable — was enough to change the philosophy of science forever. His personal life was less dramatic than his thought: a stable relationship, few friends, much work. But his thought is pure dynamite: he told science that it is not about being right, but about not clinging to what is wrong.

Core Ideas:

  1. Falsificationism (the criterion of demarcation) — For Popper, the central problem of the philosophy of science is not "how do we know a theory is true?" but "what distinguishes science from non-science?" His answer: a theory is scientific if there exist observations that can refute it. No matter how many white swans you see — the theory "all swans are white" is scientific because a single black swan topples it. Psychoanalysis and classical Marxism are not scientific because they explain everything: there is no observation that contradicts them. That does not make them false, it makes them non-falsifiable. The criterion is not one of truth, but of intellectual honesty: a theory that cannot be refuted is not a scientific theory, it is something else.
  1. Radical fallibilism — Popper does not say "we know nothing." He says: we know that what we believe we know may be wrong. And that is not a weakness — it is the only solid foundation for knowledge. If you accept that every theory is a conjecture that can be refuted, you stop clinging dogmatically to ideas. The scientific attitude is not certainty, it is epistemic humility: "this is the best we have today, but tomorrow it may fall." Error is not a failure — it is the raw material of progress. The question is not "am I right?" but "how well did it resist my attempts to refute it?"
  1. Critical rationalism — It is not a theory, it is an attitude: "I may be wrong, you may be right, and together we can get closer to the truth if we discuss openly." Rationality is not in having good reasons to believe something, it is in being willing to be refuted. Critical rationalism is the disposition to learn from one's own errors and those of others. It does not require everyone to be geniuses — it requires everyone to be willing to listen to criticism and to change their minds when arguments justify it.
  1. The open society and its enemies — In his most political book, Popper argues that societies prosper when institutions allow criticism without violence. The open society is one where ideas compete in a marketplace of refutation, not on a battlefield. Its enemies are those who believe they have absolute truth (Plato, Hegel, Marx) and want to impose it by force. Democracy is not "government by the people" — it is the only system that allows removing rulers without bloodshed. The value of democracy is not that it produces the best decisions, but that it allows correcting bad ones without violence.
  1. Against historicism — There are no laws of history. The future is not written, it cannot be predicted with scientific method. Historicism (the idea that history has a necessary and discoverable course) is a dangerous pseudoscience because it justifies present sacrifices in the name of an inevitable future. Marx, Hegel, Spengler — all historicists. Popper says: the future depends on what we do, and what we do depends on what we know. And knowledge grows in unpredictable ways. Long-term historical prediction is impossible because future knowledge (which would affect those predictions) cannot be known today.
  1. The three worlds — Popper distinguishes three ontological domains: World 1 (physical, objects), World 2 (mental, subjective), World 3 (objective knowledge: theories, books, arguments, works of art). World 3 is autonomous: once created, it has consequences no one foresaw. The theory of relativity, once written, lives independently of Einstein. Objective knowledge is not in our heads — it is in books, files, arguments. And we can criticize it without criticizing ourselves. This has an immense practical consequence: separating the idea from the person allows discussion without relationships breaking.
  1. The paradox of tolerance — "Unlimited tolerance leads to the disappearance of tolerance." If we are tolerant of the intolerant, the intolerant will destroy us. The open society must defend itself against those who want to close it. Not every opinion deserves the same respect — those that promote violence against open discussion have no right to benefit from the tolerance they seek to destroy.

Key Works:

  • The Logic of Scientific Discovery (1934/1959) — His foundational work. Falsificationism explained in earnest. It is not easy to read (Popper was not an elegant writer), but each page builds an argument that is still relevant today. Start with chapter 1 (the problem of induction) and then jump to "Conjectures and Refutations" if it gets too dense.
  • The Open Society and Its Enemies (1945) — A fierce critique of Plato, Hegel, and Marx. Written during World War II, with the urgency of someone who saw totalitarianism up close and lost family in the Holocaust. Much more accessible than his work on science. Read it for what it is: an attempt to save liberal civilization while Europe was burning.
  • Conjectures and Refutations (1963) — Collection of essays. His most readable book. Here is "Science: Conjectures and Refutations," which is the best introduction to his thought. Also contains his famous lecture on the paradox of tolerance.
  • The Poverty of Historicism (1957) — Systematic attack on the idea that history has predictable laws. Short, incisive, devastating.

Connects with:

  • Deutsch (direct and explicit heir — all of The Beginning of Infinity is Popperianism applied to the progress of knowledge as a cosmic phenomenon; Deutsch takes fallibilism and extends it to radical optimism)
  • Taleb (the black swan is falsification applied to radical uncertainty; the turkey who thinks the butcher is his friend because he never saw him kill a turkey is pure Popperian induction)
  • Feynman (the scientific method as an attitude of no self-deception — "the scientist does not try to verify his theory, he tries to refute it"; Feynman lived it without calling it falsificationism)
  • Kahneman (fallibilism applied to psychology — our biases are conjectures we do not submit to refutation; System 1 does not falsify, it confirms)
  • Munger (inverting investing — "show me where I will die and I will never go there" is pure falsification; Munger's checklist is a refutation machine)
  • Yudkowsky (rationality as active error correction; "actually updating on evidence" is Bayesian fallibilism)
  • Graham (dissent as an engine of clarity — if there is no criticism, there is no improvement; Y Combinator's culture is Popperian without knowing it)
  • Borges (fiction as conjecture — Borges creates possible worlds as hypotheses; Popper says all knowledge is conjecture)

Expanded Pocket Quotes/Ideas:

  • "There is no method for finding the truth, only methods for eliminating error" — That is why science advances by refutation, not by confirmation. Seeking evidence in favor is easy; seeking evidence against is the real work. If you only look for what confirms your idea, you are not learning — you are decorating your bias.
  • "The true scientist does not try to verify his theory, he tries to refute it" — If you only seek confirmation, you are not doing science. You are doing epistemic self-indulgence. The litmus test of an idea is whether it survived an honest attempt to tear it down.
  • "The intolerance of the tolerant destroys tolerance" — The paradox of tolerance. The open society needs limits: active intolerants are not tolerated. Being neutral toward those who want to destroy your freedom is not virtue — it is suicide.
  • "Theories are nets to catch what we call the world" — Conjectures are tools, not eternal truths. Change the tool when it does not catch. Do not fall in love with your nets.
  • "Our knowledge can only grow as long as we are not too sure of it" — Certainty is the enemy of learning. Doubt is not weakness — it is the engine. The one who is too sure has stopped learning.
  • "Science does not rest upon solid rock, but on stilts in a swamp" — Knowledge is never definitive. We are always on uncertain ground, but we can build on what has withstood us so far.

Actionable Concepts from Popper

Concept 1: Falsification in Decisions

What it is: Popper inverted the logic of knowledge: do not ask "what evidence confirms my idea?" Ask "what evidence would refute it?" A decision is solid not when it has many reasons in its favor, but when it survived attempts to tear it down. Falsification is not an academic concept — it is the most powerful filter for making decisions. If you are going to be wrong, let it be for a reason you could not see, not for not having looked.

When to use it: When evaluating an investment thesis, a business hypothesis, or any decision where confirmation bias can blind you. The moment you feel that "everything points in the same direction," that is exactly the moment to apply falsification. If you find nothing that could refute your thesis, it is not that your thesis is solid — it is that you have not examined it.

Concrete example: You are considering investing in a startup because "the market is huge, the team is incredible, the product is unique." Popper would say: perfect. Now tell me: what would have to happen for this deal to be a total failure? If the founder cannot answer, or gets offended, you have a signal. If they answer ("if we do not gain traction in the first 6 months, our CAC will kill us"), you have a falsifiable hypothesis you can monitor. The plan is not only the path to success — it is also what signals will tell you that you were wrong.

How to apply it: For any important decision, write: "This is what I believe will happen. This is what would have to happen for me to be wrong." Monitor that second scenario with more attention than the first. Most people monitor whether their thesis is confirmed — the minority who monitors whether it is refuted has the advantage.

Antithesis: Do not falsify trivial decisions (what to eat, what to read). Falsification requires cognitive effort — use it where the cost of error is high. Nor should you use it to paralyze yourself by constantly asking "what if I am wrong?" at levels where there is no clear hypothesis.

Concept 2: Error as Data, Not as Failure

What it is: For Popper, error is not a failure — it is the raw material of knowledge. Each refuted theory is not a defeat, it is progress: now you know something you did not know before (that that path does not work). The question is not "was I wrong?" — the answer is always yes at some level. The question is "did I learn something from the error?" If the answer is yes, the error was a net gain.

When to use it: When you made a mistake in an investment, a decision, an important judgment. Instead of feeling guilt or shame, ask yourself: "what conjecture did I hold that I now know is false?" That data is worth more than the reason you did not have. A portfolio of analyzed errors is an asset that appreciates over time.

Concrete example: You invested in a startup that went bankrupt. The easy narrative: "it was bad luck" or "I was deceived." The Popperian narrative: "my thesis was that X would lead to Y. X happened but Y did not. Therefore, my causal model was wrong. This is what I learned about how these dynamics really work." The error is not a net loss — it is a purchase of knowledge. The one who does not analyze their errors pays the tuition but does not attend the class.

How to apply it: Keep a "diary of refuted conjectures." Not a diary of sad failures — a record of hypotheses you held, what refuted them, and what you learned. Over time, it is your most valuable asset. When you see a pattern (e.g., "I always get B2B SaaS early-stage deals wrong"), you have a systematic refutation that can change your criteria.

Antithesis: Not every error is a useful refutation. Sometimes you are wrong due to noise (bad luck), not because your theory is bad. Popper distinguishes between systematic error (refutation of a conjecture) and random error (bad luck). Do not learn lessons where there was only noise. Also: do not fall into "error porn" — analyzing errors can become a vice. The idea is not to ruminate, it is to extract the lesson and move on.

Concept 3: Open Society in Your Organization

What it is: The open society is a system where ideas are criticized without violence. Popper applied it to politics; it applies equally to a company, a fund, a team. An open organization is where anyone can say "this is wrong" without fear of reprisal. Where arguments weigh more than titles. Where the junior can refute the senior if they have better data.

When to use it: When you see that in your team decisions are made by hierarchy and not by quality of argument. When people are afraid to dissent. When "geniuses" are not questioned. When meetings are monologues. The clearest sign that you do not have an open society: someone says "this does not work" and the response is defensive rather than curious.

Concrete example: In an investment committee, a senior partner proposes a deal. Everyone nods. The junior who has doubts does not speak because "who am I to contradict?" Popper would say: the junior should speak, and the senior should thank them. If not, you do not have an open society — you have a feudal court. Concrete solution: appoint someone as an official "devil's advocate" for each decision. Their job is to find why the thesis is wrong. Pay them for it. Change the person each time.

How to apply it: Create an explicit mechanism for dissent. Before deciding, hold a round where everyone must give their best argument against the decision. The proposer does not defend — they only listen. If after hearing the criticisms the decision still stands, it is stronger. If it falls, you saved yourself an error. There is no scenario where structured criticism worsens the decision.

Antithesis: The open society does not mean all opinions are equally valid. It means all criticisms deserve to be heard if they are well-argued. The criterion is not democratic — it is epistemic. It is not "everyone says what they think" — it is "everyone submits their idea to the criticism of others." Discussion is not noise — it is filter.

Concept 4: Strategic Anti-Historicism

What it is: Popper says there are no laws of history. You cannot predict the future based on the past. Trends are not laws. The future is built with your decisions and your knowledge, which is unpredictable. Applied to strategy: do not marry a 5-year projection. Do not believe that "this has always worked this way" is a guarantee that it will keep working.

When to use it: When someone says "historically this has always worked" as an argument to repeat the same strategy. When a business plan is based on linear extrapolation of trends. When complacency disguises itself as "experience." When you hear "that's how it's always been done" — that phrase should set off all your alarms.

Concrete example: "Marketplaces always take 5 years to become profitable." Popper: that is not a law, it is a historical trend that depended on specific conditions (cheap capital, little competition, organic user growth). If conditions change (high rates, saturated competition), the "law" does not apply. Do not assume the past repeats — assume it may not, and design your strategy to be robust across different futures. The one who confuses a trend with a law is guaranteeing their surprise.

How to apply it: When you hear "it's always been done this way" or "historically this works," ask: "what specific conditions made it work? Are they still valid today?" If you cannot answer, the historical argument is useless. Better yet: ask yourself "under what conditions would this trend break?" If you cannot imagine it, you did not understand the trend.

Antithesis: Do not confuse historicism with learning from history. Popper does not say history teaches nothing — he says there are no mechanical laws. Learn from patterns, extract general principles, but do not marry specific predictions. History is a laboratory of ideas, not an oracle.

Concept 5: World 3 — Knowledge Outside Your Head

What it is: Popper says that objective knowledge (theories, arguments, books) lives in a separate world — it is not in your mind, it is in public records. This has an immense practical consequence: you can criticize an idea without criticizing the person. If the idea is in World 3, it is a public object, not an extension of the ego of the one who proposed it. You can dissect it, modify it, refute it, improve it — without anyone feeling attacked.

When to use it: When a discussion becomes personal. When someone defends their idea as if defending their life. When you cannot separate criticism from offense. Remember: the idea is in World 3. Criticizing it is not criticizing the person. If the other person cannot make that separation, you have a culture problem, not an argument problem.

Concrete example: In a due diligence meeting, you question a colleague's thesis. He becomes defensive. Popper: "The thesis I am criticizing is not yours — it is on the whiteboard. We can modify it, replace it, improve it together. You are not your thesis." Separating the idea from the person allows discussion without relationships breaking. It is one of the most undervalued skills in organizations.

How to apply it: When a colleague becomes defensive, reframe: "I am not saying you are wrong. I am saying this hypothesis (point to the whiteboard or document) has problems. Let's improve it together." If the other person insists on personal defense, you have data on their ability to operate in an open society.

Antithesis: It is not an excuse not to take responsibility for your ideas. Ideas can be public, but you choose to defend them, abandon them, or improve them. World 3 gives you distance, it does not exempt you from accountability.

Concept 6: Tolerance with Limits

What it is: The paradox of tolerance: if you are tolerant of everything, the intolerant will destroy you. Tolerance must have limits: active intolerance, violence, censorship are not tolerated. The open society must defend itself against those who want to close it. The limit of tolerance is tolerance itself.

When to use it: When someone uses "it's my opinion" as a shield to say anything without possibility of criticism. When you have to decide whether to give a platform to an idea that promotes closing discussion. When neutrality becomes complicity. When "respect for all opinions" is used to protect opinions that respect no one.

Concrete example: In your fund, a partner proposes not to invest in founders of a certain profile based on a stereotype. He says "it's my opinion, I have a right." Popper: no, tolerance does not protect opinions that close the door to others. The criterion is not "it's my opinion" — it is "can this idea be openly criticized?" If it cannot be questioned (because it is based on a non-falsifiable prejudice), it has no place in an organization that depends on critical thinking.

How to apply it: Define red lines in your organization: "arguments that cannot be questioned are not tolerated" or "opinions based on verifiably false data with intent to harm are not tolerated." It is not censorship — it is defending the conditions of open discussion. The limit is not "what can be said" but "how it can be discussed."

Antithesis: Do not confuse intolerance with discomfort. An uncomfortable idea is not intolerant. Discomfort is part of open discussion — in fact, it is a sign that something important is happening. Intolerance is closing the possibility of discussion. Learn to distinguish: discomfort makes you think; intolerance makes you shut down.

How to Reinforce Popper

Weekly routine (10 min):

  1. Choose a belief you hold (about investing, a person, how something works)
  2. Write: "This is what I believe. This is what would have to happen for me to be wrong."
  3. Actively seek evidence that contradicts your belief (an article, a data point, a conversation)
  4. If you find something, do not discard it — ask yourself "does this refute my belief or only qualify it?"
  5. Update. If the belief changed, note what you learned.

Deepening routine (monthly):

  • Read "Conjectures and Refutations" — especially chapter 1 ("Science: Conjectures and Refutations") and the chapter on the paradox of tolerance
  • Then read "The Beginning of Infinity" by Deutsch — it is Popperianism applied to the 21st century, physics, AI, progress
  • Watch the Popper vs. Wittgenstein debate (exists on YouTube, fragments) — Popper's passion in arguing shows that criticism is not cold, it is passionate

Practical exercise: In your next investment meeting, after presenting the thesis, hold a "kill the thesis" round. Each person has 2 minutes to give the best reason NOT to invest. The person presenting the thesis does not defend — they only listen and take notes. Then, review: did the thesis survive? Or was it killed? If it was killed, better to kill it with words than with real money.

Do not accumulate:

  • You do not need to read all of Popper's work to use him. Understanding falsificationism, fallibilism, and the paradox of tolerance gives you powerful tools for life.
  • The danger with Popper is using him to criticize everything without building anything. Misunderstood fallibilism leads to cynicism: "everything could be wrong, so nothing matters." Criticism is the means, not the end. The goal is to improve ideas, not just tear them down.

Chapter 7Charlie Munger

Life: Charles Thomas Munger (1924–2023), American investor, lawyer, and practical philosopher. Vice Chairman of Berkshire Hathaway, Warren Buffett's partner for over 50 years. Born in Omaha, Nebraska, he studied mathematics at the University of Michigan, enlisted in the army during World War II (where he learned to think under pressure), and later studied law at Harvard without having completed his undergraduate degree. He practiced law, founded his own firm, and began investing on the side. He met Buffett in 1959; the encounter changed the history of investing. Initially, Buffett followed Benjamin Graham (buy undervalued assets, regardless of quality). Munger pushed him toward a different approach: buy quality companies at fair prices, not mediocre companies at liquidation prices. That philosophical shift transformed Berkshire from a troubled fund into one of the most valuable conglomerates in the world. Munger is famous not only for his results as an investor, but for how he thought. His most enduring contribution isn't a portfolio — it's a mental attitude: the latticework of mental models, the psychology of human judgment, and the insistence that rationality is a moral duty. He spoke with brutal frankness, didn't use PowerPoint, read 6 hours a day into his 90s, and said the secret of life was "to have a list of stupid things and not do them." He died at 99, going to work until the very end.

Core ideas:

  1. The latticework of mental models — For Munger, you can't understand the world with a single discipline. You need about 80–100 mental models from different fields — physics, biology, psychology, history, economics, mathematics — and you need to know when to apply each one. The problem of the "man with a hammer" (to whom everything looks like a nail) is the most common trap in thinking. An investor who only knows finance is blind to psychology, systems dynamics, game theory, and evolutionary biology. The latticework isn't general culture — it's a network of interconnected tools to avoid making stupid mistakes. Each model is a lens; with a single lens you see a caricature of the world, with many you see the real complexity.
  1. Invert, always invert — "Show me where I'll die and I'll never go there." Munger learned from mathematicians: if you want to solve a problem, sometimes the best way is to turn it upside down. Don't ask "how do I succeed?" — ask "how do I ensure failure?" and avoid all those things. It's easier to avoid stupidity than to seek brilliance. This idea (which Taleb popularized as the "via negativa") is central: the most useful knowledge isn't what to do, it's what to avoid. A list of what not to do is more valuable than a list of what to do, because the former eliminates errors while the latter only suggests actions.
  1. The psychology of human error — Munger studied cognitive biases before Kahneman popularized them. His famous list includes 25 biases: confirmation bias, Dunning-Kruger effect, perverse incentives (the principle that if you put out a carrot and a stick, people respond to the incentive even if it's destructive), availability bias, mental accounting, halo effect, authority bias, and dozens more. His genius wasn't discovering them, but building a practical system to mitigate them. It's not enough to know they exist — you need checklists, routines, and mechanisms so they don't catch you off guard.
  1. Circle of competence — "Know what you know and what you don't. Act only inside." Munger says most errors in life come from operating outside your circle of competence. It's not false humility — it's epistemic precision. If you don't understand a business, no matter how good it looks — pass. The circle can be expanded, but slowly, through systematic study. Meanwhile, the person who knows they don't know and acts accordingly has a huge advantage over the person who thinks they know and doesn't. The circle of competence isn't a limitation — it's a protection.
  1. Patience and radical concentration — Munger and Buffett made most of their fortune with just a few decisions. They didn't diversify for the sake of diversifying — they waited for a "no-brainer bet" to appear and then bet big. "Investing isn't a sport where the one who's right the most often wins. The one who wins is the one who's right big when they really have an advantage." Patience isn't passivity — it's sitting still, doing nothing, completely ready to move when the right opportunity appears. Most investors feel pressured to "do something"; Munger made a fortune doing almost nothing most of the time.
  1. Incentives as superpower — "Show me the incentive and I'll show you the outcome." Munger learned from biology and economics: organisms respond to incentives in predictable ways. If you want to understand why someone does something, don't look at their stated values — look at their real incentives. A fund salesman doesn't get paid if you don't invest, no matter how much he swears "this is what's best for you." A lawyer bills by the hour, not by results. A CEO is compensated with stock, not dividends. Incentives explain more about behavior than any personality theory.
  1. Rationality as a moral duty — For Munger, thinking well isn't an option — it's an obligation. "The duty to be rational." It's not a cold intellectual posture — it's a deep conviction. If you have the capacity to think clearly, you have the responsibility to use it, especially when others depend on you (investors, employees, family). Irrationality (bias, unchecked emotion, magical thinking) isn't just a mistake — it's an ethical failure when it has consequences for others. Rationality isn't a lifestyle choice, it's a requirement of the craft of living in society.

Key works:

  • Poor Charlie's Almanack (2005) — The Munger bible. A compilation of his speeches, essays, and aphorisms. Includes "The Psychology of Human Misjudgment," his catalog of 25 biases. If you read only one investing book in your life, let it be this one. It's not read in one sitting — you chew on one speech per week.
  • The Psychology of Human Misjudgment (1995) — A standalone text (included in the Almanack). His list of 25 cognitive biases with practical examples. More useful than many academic psychology books because Munger doesn't just describe — he shows how to hunt them in practice.
  • Seeking Wisdom: From Darwin to Munger (2007) by Peter Bevelin — Not by Munger, but the best summary and systematization of his method. Bevelin organizes Munger's mental models with pedagogical clarity.
  • Damn Right! (2000) by Janet Lowe — Authorized biography. Shows the man behind the quotes.

Connects with:

  • Buffett (they're not the same — Munger is more philosophical and systematic, Buffett more pragmatic and intuitive; together they're the yin and yang of value investing; Munger pushed Buffett to buy quality, Buffett gave Munger the platform to be a philosopher)
  • Taleb (via negativa — both understand that avoiding errors is more important than seeking successes; antifragility — Munger also looks for systems that benefit from chaos and volatility)
  • Kahneman (the psychology of biases; Munger applied it empirically decades before Kahneman validated it scientifically; Kahneman provides the theory, Munger the street-level practice)
  • Feynman (no self-deception — same insistence on intellectual honesty as a duty; both despise empty jargon and authority without evidence)
  • Popper (falsification — Munger's inversion is applied falsification: seek to refute your thesis before executing it; Munger's checklist is a falsification machine)
  • Deutsch (knowledge as problem-solving — Munger would say mental models are objective knowledge in Popper's sense; both optimistic about human progress based on knowledge)
  • Graham (Benjamin Graham — the father of value investing; Munger surpassed him by adding "quality" to the "price" criterion)
  • Yudkowsky (Bayesian rationality, biases, active error correction — Munger would have been a great lesswronger)
  • Marcus Aurelius (practical Stoicism — rationality as duty, acceptance of chance, focus on what you control)

Expanded pocket quotes/ideas:

  • "If you're not a little confused by what you do, you don't understand it" — The complexity of the world is real. If your explanation is too simple, you've probably left out something key. Confusion isn't a sign of failure — it's a sign you're dealing with real complexity.
  • "Show me where I'll die and I'll never go there" — Invert, always invert. Apply this to investments, strategies, relationships, habits. First ask "what kills me?" and then avoid that.
  • "The best trick in life is to have a list of stupid things and not do them" — More useful than having a list of smart things to do. The list of what not to do eliminates paths to failure; the list of what to do only suggests paths to success.
  • "The duty to be rational" — Thinking well isn't optional when others depend on you. Rationality is a moral responsibility, not an intellectual preference.
  • "All I know is that most of the people I know who are happier than me have broad and varied interests" — Happiness as a byproduct of curiosity, not accumulation.
  • "Money isn't happiness, but it lets you be unhappy in more comfortable places" — Brutal honesty about what money can and can't do.
  • "If you want to get smarter, the recipe is simple: read a lot. There are no shortcuts" — He read 6 hours a day. It wasn't a slogan; it was how he lived. If you don't read, you don't have models; if you don't have models, you're the man with the hammer.

Actionable Concepts from Munger

Concept 1: The Hammer and Mental Models

What it is: Munger says the "man with a hammer" sees nails everywhere. If you only have one mental model (finance, for example), you apply that model to everything — and you're systematically wrong. The solution is to have an arsenal of models from different disciplines: physics (equilibrium, tipping point, critical mass), biology (evolution, incentives, natural selection), psychology (biases, conditioning), economics (comparative advantage, sunk costs, economies of scale), game theory (cooperation, prisoner's dilemma, Nash equilibrium). It's not about knowing everything — it's about having enough lenses not to apply the same one to fundamentally different problems.

When to use it: When you realize you're using the same framework for everything. If your answer to any problem is "it's a matter of incentives" (psychology) or "it's a matter of scale" (economics) or "it's a matter of culture" (anthropology), you're probably hammering. The world is more varied than your favorite discipline.

Concrete example: You're analyzing a subscription startup with high customer churn. The financial investor's instinct: "they need a better product" (a single model). Munger: apply multiple models. Is it an incentive problem (sales team gets paid for new customers, not retention)? Is it a biology problem (the company grew so fast it exceeded its carrying capacity)? Is it a physics problem (churn is the natural entropy of the system and you need constant energy to maintain order)? Is it a psychology problem (users try it, get bored, and there are no switching costs)? Each model gives you a different intervention. The one who only knows finance sees "raise prices." The one who knows psychology sees "create a habit." The one who knows biology sees "slow growth."

How to apply it: When facing a problem, force yourself to find at least three models from different disciplines. Ask yourself: "how would a biologist see this? A psychologist? A physicist? A poker player?" If you only have one perspective, you're the man with the hammer.

Antithesis: Don't use mental models to decorate conversations. A misapplied model is worse than no model at all. Knowing when NOT to apply a model is as important as knowing when to apply it. Also: don't accumulate models without practicing them — 10 well-integrated models are worth more than 50 memorized ones.

Concept 2: Invert, Always Invert (Via Negativa)

What it is: Instead of asking "what do I need to do to succeed?", ask "what things will guarantee failure?" and avoid all of them. It's easier to avoid stupidity than to seek brilliance. The via negativa isn't pessimism — it's efficiency: eliminating what doesn't work is a shorter path than inventing what does.

When to use it: When facing a complex decision with many variables. When the positive approach ("what to do") gives you too many options and you don't know where to start. When you feel no option is clearly better. The inverse question usually has a clearer, more actionable answer.

Concrete example: You're deciding whether to partner with a co-investor or business partner. Instead of asking "what qualities do they have?" (generic answer: smart, experienced, etc.), ask "what would make this partnership fail?" Lack of transparency, misaligned incentives, incompatible egos, opposite communication styles, different risk tolerance. If you identify any of these on the list, no matter how good the rest is — pass. Avoiding a toxic partnership is more important than finding the perfect one.

How to apply it: For any important decision, write two lists: (1) what to do, (2) what to avoid. Spend more hours on the second one. One hour avoiding stupid mistakes is worth more than ten hours looking for the brilliant move. The list of what to avoid is shorter, clearer, and more actionable.

Antithesis: The via negativa isn't inaction. It's not about doing nothing — it's about eliminating what doesn't work so that what does work has space. It's a strategy of clearing, not of stillness. Nor is it cynicism: eliminating errors doesn't mean distrusting everything.

Concept 3: Incentives as a Lens

What it is: Munger repeats: "Show me the incentive and I'll show you the outcome." Incentives are the most powerful force in human behavior. If you want to understand why someone does what they do, look at their real incentives, not their stated words. Most people don't consciously lie — but their incentives push them in directions they themselves don't see. Conflict of incentives doesn't require bad faith; bad faith is a bonus.

When to use it: When someone gives you advice (a broker, an advisor, a partner, a friend). When a team or partner doesn't behave as you expected. When a deal seems too good to be true. Always ask: "what are this person's incentives? Are they aligned with mine or in conflict?"

Concrete example: A financial advisor recommends a fund with high fees. He says "it's the best." Munger: the advisor's incentive is to earn commission, not to maximize your returns. He's not necessarily lying — but his brain filters information so that the best option for him seems like the best option for you. The conflict of incentives is structural, not personal. The solution isn't to change advisors — it's to change the incentive structure (pay for results, not commissions).

How to apply it: In any relationship where someone advises you, make the incentives explicit before listening to the advice: "how do you benefit if I do this? How do you benefit if I don't?" If the answer isn't clear or reveals a conflict, be wary. Not of the advisor — of the structure. And if you can, redesign the structure.

Antithesis: Incentives don't explain everything. There are people who act on values, ethics, pride, or identity. But assume the incentive first — if the behavior is explained without appealing to virtue, virtue is probably not the driver. It's not cynical, it's realistic.

Concept 4: Circle of Competence

What it is: Your circle of competence consists of the areas where you truly understand how things work. Not what you think you know, not what you studied 10 years ago, not what you can Google in 5 minutes. What you understand so deeply that you can predict consequences with high reliability. Munger says: operate only inside. Outside: don't opine, don't invest, don't decide.

When to use it: When you're tempted to invest in something "because it's a good opportunity" even though you don't fully understand it. When you're in a conversation about a topic you don't master and feel social pressure to have an opinion. When a deal has a thesis you can't explain in your own words without jargon.

Concrete example: A biotech deal comes along. The market is huge, preliminary results are promising, the team has Harvard PhDs. But you don't understand molecular biology or clinical trials. Munger: pass. No matter how good the opportunity looks — if it's outside your circle, it's not for you. The market will have other opportunities that you CAN evaluate. It's not that you never invest in biotech — it's that today it's not your game. Expand your circle by studying biotech, or accept that it's not for you.

How to apply it: Make an honest list of what IS inside your circle of competence. Be brutally honest. For everything else, the correct answer is "I don't know enough to have an opinion." That phrase doesn't make you less competent — it makes you more trustworthy. People who never say "I don't know" are the least trustworthy of all.

Antithesis: The circle can expand. It's not an excuse not to learn or to encapsulate yourself. The question isn't "is it in my circle?" but "am I willing to invest the time necessary to get IT in my circle?" If the answer is yes, study before deciding. If it's no, don't opine.

Concept 5: Active Patience

What it is: Munger and Buffett spent decades sitting still, doing almost nothing, waiting. Not because they were lazy — because they understood that most of the time there are no exceptional opportunities. And when they appear, you need to be ready to move fast and hard. "Most of life is waiting. The key is knowing what to wait for." Patience isn't inaction — it's active preparation for the right moment.

When to use it: When you feel the pressure to "do something" — invest in something just to stay active, make a deal just to justify your existence or your fee. When the market is calm and everyone is nervous. When your instinct tells you to "take advantage" of mediocre opportunities because "there's nothing better."

Concrete example: You're at a fund and haven't found a deal that convinces you in 6 months. Your LPs ask when you'll deploy capital. Your partner is impatient. Munger: it doesn't matter. A bad investment is worse than no investment. Wait. The pressure to deploy capital is the biggest enemy of returns. The one who waits for the right deal has an insurmountable advantage over the one who desperately needs to do something. Buffett once said: "Most of my money was made in years when I did almost nothing."

How to apply it: Define clear investment criteria in advance and don't bend them because "there's nothing better." If it doesn't meet the criteria, pass. It doesn't matter how much time goes by. The market will always give you another opportunity if you're alive, have capital, and keep looking.

Antithesis: Patience isn't procrastination on steroids. Don't sit still without studying, without preparing, without expanding your circle of competence. Active patience is waiting + preparing. Passive patience is just waiting. The difference is everything.

Concept 6: Mental Checklist

What it is: Munger took the idea of the checklist from medicine and aviation. Pilots don't trust their memory — they have a list of things to check before every takeoff. Surgeons do too. In investing, where bias and emotion systematically cloud judgment, a checklist is the difference between a rational decision and a rationalization (a decision that seems rational but only justifies what you already wanted to do).

When to use it: Before any important investment decision. Before a critical meeting. Before sending an email that could be contentious. Any situation where pressure, ego, or time might make you skip steps. If the decision is important, it needs a checklist.

Concrete example: You have a deal on the table. The founder is charismatic, the market is hot, you want to close. Munger's checklist: (1) Is this business inside my circle of competence? (2) What are the real incentives of the founding team? (3) What would have to happen for this to be a failure? (4) Am I being influenced by availability bias (it's the last deal I saw and it's fresh)? (5) Can I explain the thesis in 3 minutes to a non-expert? If any answer makes you hesitate, don't sign — resolve the doubt first.

How to apply it: Create your own checklist for each type of important decision. Don't change it impulsively after each decision — review it periodically (every 6 months). The checklist protects you from yourself, who is the easiest person to fool. Without a checklist, you're operating with System 1 as the pilot.

Antithesis: The checklist doesn't replace judgment or experience. It's a safety net, not an autopilot. If something isn't on the list but it feels wrong, listen to that feeling — it could be your experience speaking beyond the predefined items. The checklist is a floor, not a ceiling.

How to Reinforce Munger

Weekly routine (15 min):

  1. Choose a situation from the week where you made a decision (investment, hiring, strategy, personal)
  2. Identify what mental model you used (incentives? via negativa? circle of competence?)
  3. Identify what models you did NOT use and could have helped
  4. Ask yourself: "what bias might have influenced my decision?"
  5. Write the answer in a decision journal. Over time, you'll see patterns.

Deepening routine (monthly):

  • Read ONE speech from Poor Charlie's Almanack. Don't read it all at once — one chapter per month, digested. The best ones: "A Lesson on Elementary, Worldly Wisdom," "The Psychology of Human Misjudgment," and his speech on how to make your life a living hell.
  • After reading, write down how you apply that idea to your life or work right now. If you can't find an application, reread it — you missed something.
  • Read something OUTSIDE your discipline: evolutionary biology (Dawkins), physics (Feynman), history (Tuchman), psychology (Kahneman). Not to become an expert, but to add models.

Practical exercise: The next time you have to make an important decision, write a 5-item checklist specific to THAT type of decision. Use it. Then review: did it work? What items were missing? What items were unnecessary? Iterate. Over time, your checklist will become more accurate than your intuition.

Don't accumulate:

  • Don't try to learn all 100 mental models at once. Start with 5-10 and use them until they're automatic and natural. Add one new one each month, not each day.
  • The danger of Munger is quoting him without practicing him. The quotes are brilliant and quotable, but the method is the real work. There's no shortcut. Reading Munger quotes doesn't make you Munger — applying his checklists every day does.

Chapter 8Daniel Kahneman

Life: Daniel Kahneman (1934–2024), Israeli-American psychologist and Nobel Prize winner in Economics (2002) without having formally studied economics. Born in Tel Aviv, he grew up in Paris during World War II — his Jewish family lived hidden from the Nazis, used false documents, changed names. That early experience — the need to detect threats where there might be none, distrust of appearances, the awareness that the mind can deceive you — probably seeded his obsession with how the mind systematically goes wrong. He studied psychology at the Hebrew University of Jerusalem and later earned his PhD at Berkeley. His career changed forever in 1969, when he met Amos Tversky. Together, Kahneman and Tversky revolutionized the psychology of human judgment and decision-making under uncertainty. They demonstrated that humans are not rational in the classical economic sense — we are half-rational, biased, predictably irrational. Their experiments (the Linda problem, anchoring, loss aversion) are today the most cited in the social sciences. Kahneman won the Nobel in 2002; Tversky had died in 1996, and the Nobel is not awarded posthumously, but Kahneman always said the Nobel belonged to them both. His book Thinking, Fast and Slow (2011) is one of the most important books of the 21st century on how the human mind works. It's not a self-help manual — it's an autopsy of rationality. Kahneman doesn't tell you how to be happy; he tells you why you think you're rational when you're not.

Core ideas:

  1. System 1 and System 2 — The brain has two modes of operation. System 1: fast, automatic, intuitive, emotional, effortless. System 2: slow, deliberate, analytical, effortful, lazy. System 1 is what makes you brake when you see a shadow on the road. System 2 is what solves 17 × 24. Most of our decisions are made by System 1, and System 2 ratifies them afterward, like a manager signing off on what their assistant already did. The problem isn't that System 1 exists — it's that System 2 is lazy and doesn't correct System 1's errors. Most people live in System 1 and believe they're in System 2.
  1. Systematic cognitive biases — We don't make mistakes at random. We make mistakes in predictable, systematic ways. Kahneman and Tversky showed that judgment errors aren't individual failures — they're features of the mental hardware, shortcuts (heuristics) that work well most of the time but fail predictably in specific contexts. Examples: confirmation bias (we seek evidence that supports what we already believe), availability (we judge probability by how easily we recall an example), representativeness (we judge by resemblance, not base rates). Knowing about these biases doesn't eliminate them — but it allows you to design systems that mitigate them.
  1. Loss aversion — Losing hurts about twice as much as winning pleases. This isn't folk wisdom, it's a robust experimental finding, replicated hundreds of times. A loss of $100 feels like a gain of $200 unrealized. This explains massive irrational patterns: why people don't sell assets that are falling (they wait to recover because selling is "realizing" the loss), why investors cling to losing positions, why the status quo is so hard to change, why bad news weighs more than good news in organizations.
  1. Anchoring — The first number you're exposed to conditions everything that follows. If I tell you a fund's average return is 20% and ask you what this one might return, you'll answer around 20%. If I say 5%, you answer near 5%. The anchor doesn't have to be relevant — it works even if it's arbitrary (the last digit of your ID number, a random number). Kahneman showed that anchoring operates even when you know it exists and know it's arbitrary. You can't voluntarily "de-anchor" yourself; the only defense is to have your own number before being exposed to someone else's anchor.
  1. Noise vs. Bias — In his last book, Kahneman distinguishes two types of error: bias (systematic error, always in the same direction) and noise (random error, inconsistency). Bias is famous; noise is more treacherous because it's invisible. If several professionals give different answers to the same problem, there is noise. And noise is just as damaging as bias — sometimes more, because it goes unseen and unmeasured. Kahneman showed that in many professions (judges giving different sentences for the same case, doctors diagnosing the same patient differently, executives evaluating the same candidate differently), there is more noise than anyone wants to admit. Noise isn't solved by training — it's solved by structured processes.
  1. The halo effect — First impressions contaminate everything else. If a CEO seems competent (good presence, speaks well, has charisma), we assume they're also a good strategist, good leader, good person, good financial manager. One positive quality colors all other evaluations. In investing, the halo effect makes a charismatic founder get a higher valuation than a shy one with better underlying metrics. The only defense is to evaluate each dimension separately, independently, before forming a global judgment.
  1. Two types of experience — Kahneman distinguishes between domains where experience generates genuine expertise (chess, piloting, anesthesia, high-performance sports) and domains where experience is illusory (stock market investing, personnel selection, political diagnosis, management). In the first, feedback is fast, clear, and repetitive: you won or lost, the patient woke up or didn't, you landed well or poorly. In the second, feedback is slow, noisy, and ambiguous — and the expert's confidence doesn't correlate with their actual accuracy. This distinction is key: know which domain you're in before trusting your intuition. If you're in a noisy domain, you need processes, not intuition.

Key works:

  • Thinking, Fast and Slow (2011) — The foundational book. All of Kahneman in one volume. Divided into 5 parts: (1) the two systems, (2) heuristics and biases, (3) overconfidence, (4) choices and prospect theory, and (5) the experiencing self vs. the remembering self. It's not a fast read — each chapter deserves a pause to digest the experiment and its implications. Start with the chapter on loss aversion (Part 4) if you want to go straight to what's most useful for investing.
  • Noise: A Flaw in Human Judgment (2021) — With Cass Sunstein and Olivier Sibony. The necessary complement to Thinking, Fast and Slow. It shows that noise (inconsistency between evaluators or by the same evaluator at different times) is as big a problem as bias, and provides concrete tools to reduce it.
  • Judgment Under Uncertainty: Heuristics and Biases (1982) — Edited with Amos Tversky. The original academic collection. The papers that changed psychology. Not for reading in one sitting, but having it available is like having the primary source of the knowledge.

Connects with:

  • Munger (the psychology of biases — Munger applied it empirically before Kahneman demonstrated it scientifically; Kahneman gave it experimental rigor and academic legitimacy; Munger provides the checklists, Kahneman provides the experiments that justify them)
  • Taleb (judgment errors, overconfidence, the problem of evidence — Taleb extends Kahneman's critique to financial markets)
  • Feynman (no self-deception — Kahneman explains the mechanism by which it's so hard; System 1 is the perfect self-deception machine; Feynman says "don't fool yourself," Kahneman says "here's why it's inevitable and how to mitigate it")
  • Popper (fallibilism — Kahneman shows that without correction systems, fallibilism is just theory; biases are systematic errors that must be corrected at the process level, not the intention level)
  • Yudkowsky (Bayesian rationality, active training against biases — Yudkowsky takes Kahneman and turns it into a training program)
  • Deutsch (knowledge as error correction — Kahneman shows the most common and persistent errors of the human hardware)
  • Graham (clarity and honesty — the fight against the halo effect and presentation biases in written communication)

Expanded pocket quotes/ideas:

  • "We are not thinking machines, we are feeling machines that occasionally think" — System 1 is the absolute default. System 2 activates with effort and only when necessary (or when you force it). Most of the time we feel, we don't think. Accepting this is the first step to designing systems that protect us.
  • "Confidence is a feeling, not a datum" — The certainty with which someone says something has no relation to the probability it's true. Confidence is an emotion generated by the coherence of the internal narrative, not by the accuracy of the data.
  • "Don't trust anyone who isn't often wrong" — If someone never admits mistakes, it's not that they don't make them — it's that they don't see them. And if they don't see them, they can't correct them. Visible error is a sign of epistemic health.
  • "What you see is all there is (WYSIATI)" — System 1 operates with the information available as if there were no more. It doesn't look for what's missing. It builds the best possible story with what it has, without asking what it might not be seeing.
  • "Nothing in life is as important as you think it is while you're thinking about it" — Attentional focus distorts the subjective importance of everything. When you devote a lot of attention to something, it feels bigger than it really is in the overall scheme of your life.
  • "Intuition is nothing more than recognition" — Intuition works well only when the pattern has repeated many times with fast, clear feedback (chess, piloting). In any other case, intuition is bias disguised as wisdom.

Actionable Concepts from Kahneman

Concept 1: Identifying System 1 vs. System 2

What it is: System 1 is your brain's autopilot. It lets you walk, talk, recognize faces, brake at danger, all without conscious effort. It also makes you judge a person by their appearance, buy on impulse, and believe a stock will go up because "it looks good." System 2 is what does calculations, weighs pros and cons, and asks "does this make sense?" The problem: System 2 is lazy and only turns on when System 1 doesn't know what to do. Most of the time we live in System 1 and think we're in System 2.

When to use it: When you're making an important decision and it feels "easy," "natural," "obvious" — that's a red flag. Easy decisions are the most suspicious. If there's no mental effort, you're probably in System 1. Also when you're tired, distracted, rushed, or emotional — those are states of high vulnerability to System 1.

Concrete example: You're evaluating a pitch. The founder is charismatic, the demo worked perfectly, the growth numbers are impressive. Your instinct: "this is incredible, let's invest." That's pure System 1. Kahneman: force System 2. Do the inverse calculation: are the growth numbers sustainable with the current unit economics? What would happen if CAC goes up 2x? How much runway do they actually have? If the pitcher is very good, your System 1 is falling asleep. Force it to wake up.

How to apply it: For important decisions, set a simple rule: don't decide the same day. Sleep on it. If after 24 hours the decision still seems obvious, only then purposefully activate System 2: write down the counterarguments, the missing data, the biases that might be operating. The 24-hour pause is the most undervalued tool for rationality.

Antithesis: Not everything requires System 2. For routine, low-impact decisions, System 1 is perfect and efficient. If you analyze every choice (what to eat for breakfast, which route to take) like it's a doctoral thesis, you'll paralyze yourself. The key is knowing when the cost of an error justifies activating System 2. General rule: irreversible or high-impact decisions deserve System 2.

Concept 2: Loss Aversion in Decisions

What it is: Losing hurts twice as much as winning pleases. This isn't an exaggeration — it's an empirical finding replicated in hundreds of experiments. This distorts decisions massively and predictably: people avoid selling assets that are down (because realizing the loss hurts more than the potential gain from reinvesting), prefer options that avoid losses even if they sacrifice equivalent gains, and cling to the status quo because losing the familiar frightens more than gaining the unknown.

When to use it: When you feel you're avoiding a decision you know is correct because it "hurts." When you don't want to sell a position that's down even though you wouldn't defend it if you were entering today. When you prefer to do nothing even though doing something is objectively better. When an LP or partner says "I don't want to lose" instead of "I want to win."

Concrete example: You have an investment that's dropped 30%. Your instinct: wait for it to recover so you can sell "at zero" (or "at break-even"). Kahneman: loss aversion is making you hold a position you wouldn't defend if you were entering with cash today. The right question isn't "how much did I lose?" — the past is irretrievable. The question is "if I had the cash today, would I invest in this with my current knowledge?" If the answer is no, sell, even if it hurts. The pain of realizing the loss is the enemy of rational decision-making.

How to apply it: For any position or project in loss, ask yourself: "If I had nothing invested here, would I invest today with my current knowledge?" If the answer is no, the decision is clear. The fact that you already have money or time there (sunk cost) shouldn't matter. It hurts, but it's correct.

Antithesis: Loss aversion is also useful — it protects you from unnecessary risks. It's not about ignoring it, it's about recognizing when it's protecting you (not taking stupid risks) and when it's blinding you (not making correct decisions that hurt). The key is distinguishing between prudent risk and paralysis from pain.

Concept 3: Conscious Anchoring

What it is: The first number you're exposed to conditions your judgment automatically and inevitably. If I tell you the valuation range is $20–30M, your brain anchors there. You can't voluntarily ignore the anchor — even when you know it's arbitrary. Your only defense is to have YOUR own number before being exposed to someone else's anchor.
